Problem: Solve the differential equation \( \frac{dy}{dx}=\frac{x^2+2xy-y^2}{x^2-2xy-y^2} \) with the initial condition \( y(1)=-1 \). Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: 0. \boxed{0}

Reference Answer: Okay, so I need to solve this differential equation: \( \frac{dy}{dx} = \frac{x^2 + 2xy - y^2}{x^2 - 2xy - y^2} \) with the initial condition \( y(1) = -1 \). Hmm, let's start by analyzing the equation to see what kind it is and what method might work here. First, both the numerator and the denominator are quadratic in x and y. That makes me think it might be a homogeneous equation. Let me check if that's true. A differential equation is homogeneous if it can be written as \( \frac{dy}{dx} = F\left( \frac{y}{x} \right) \). So let's see if substituting \( v = \frac{y}{x} \) would simplify things. Let me rewrite the equation. The numerator is \( x^2 + 2xy - y^2 \) and the denominator is \( x^2 - 2xy - y^2 \). Let me factor out \( x^2 \) from both numerator and denominator: Numerator: \( x^2 \left( 1 + 2\left( \frac{y}{x} \right) - \left( \frac{y}{x} \right)^2 \right) \) Denominator: \( x^2 \left( 1 - 2\left( \frac{y}{x} \right) - \left( \frac{y}{x} \right)^2 \right) \) So when I divide them, the \( x^2 \) terms cancel out, and I get: \( \frac{dy}{dx} = \frac{1 + 2v - v^2}{1 - 2v - v^2} \), where \( v = \frac{y}{x} \). Alright, so this substitution simplifies the equation. Now, since it's homogeneous, we can use the substitution \( y = vx \), which implies \( \frac{dy}{dx} = v + x \frac{dv}{dx} \). Let me write that down: \( v + x \frac{dv}{dx} = \frac{1 + 2v - v^2}{1 - 2v - v^2} \) So now, I need to solve for \( v \). Let's rearrange the equation to isolate \( \frac{dv}{dx} \): \( x \frac{dv}{dx} = \frac{1 + 2v - v^2}{1 - 2v - v^2} - v \) Let me compute the right-hand side. To subtract these fractions, I need a common denominator: \( \frac{1 + 2v - v^2 - v(1 - 2v - v^2)}{1 - 2v - v^2} \) Expanding the numerator: First term: \( 1 + 2v - v^2 \) Second term: \( -v + 2v^2 + v^3 \) Combine them: 1 + 2v - v^2 - v + 2v^2 + v^3 Combine like terms: 1 + (2v - v) + (-v^2 + 2v^2) + v^3 Which simplifies to: 1 + v + v^2 + v^3 So the equation becomes: \( x \frac{dv}{dx} = \frac{v^3 + v^2 + v + 1}{1 - 2v - v^2} \) Hmm, okay. Let me write that as: \( \frac{dv}{dx} = \frac{v^3 + v^2 + v + 1}{x(1 - 2v - v^2)} \) So this is now a separable equation. I can separate variables by moving all terms involving v to one side and terms involving x to the other side. So: \( \frac{1 - 2v - v^2}{v^3 + v^2 + v + 1} dv = \frac{1}{x} dx \) Now, I need to integrate both sides. The right side is straightforward: \( \int \frac{1}{x} dx = \ln|x| + C \). The left side is more complicated. Let me focus on integrating \( \frac{1 - 2v - v^2}{v^3 + v^2 + v + 1} dv \). First, let's factor the denominator if possible. Let me try to factor \( v^3 + v^2 + v + 1 \). Factor by grouping: \( v^3 + v^2 + v + 1 = v^2(v + 1) + 1(v + 1) = (v^2 + 1)(v + 1) \) Yes! So denominator factors as \( (v + 1)(v^2 + 1) \). That helps. So the integrand becomes: \( \frac{1 - 2v - v^2}{(v + 1)(v^2 + 1)} dv \) Now, let me perform partial fraction decomposition on this rational function. Let's write: \( \frac{1 - 2v - v^2}{(v + 1)(v^2 + 1)} = \frac{A}{v + 1} + \frac{Bv + C}{v^2 + 1} \) Multiply both sides by \( (v + 1)(v^2 + 1) \): \( 1 - 2v - v^2 = A(v^2 + 1) + (Bv + C)(v + 1) \) Let me expand the right-hand side: A(v^2 + 1) + Bv(v + 1) + C(v + 1) = A v^2 + A + B v^2 + B v + C v + C = (A + B) v^2 + (B + C) v + (A + C) Set this equal to the left-hand side: \( -v^2 - 2v + 1 \) So equate coefficients: For \( v^2 \): A + B = -1 For \( v \): B + C = -2 For constant term: A + C = 1 Now, we have a system of equations: 1. A + B = -1 2. B + C = -2 3. A + C = 1 Let me solve this system. Let's subtract equation 3 from equation 1: (A + B) - (A + C) = -1 - 1 => B - C = -2 But from equation 2: B + C = -2 So we have: B - C = -2 B + C = -2 Adding these two equations: 2B = -4 => B = -2 Then from equation 2: -2 + C = -2 => C = 0 From equation 3: A + 0 = 1 => A = 1 So the partial fractions decomposition is: \( \frac{1}{v + 1} + \frac{-2v}{v^2 + 1} \) Therefore, the integral becomes: \( \int \left( \frac{1}{v + 1} - \frac{2v}{v^2 + 1} \right) dv \) Let's integrate term by term: 1. \( \int \frac{1}{v + 1} dv = \ln|v + 1| + C_1 \) 2. \( \int \frac{2v}{v^2 + 1} dv \). Let me substitute u = v^2 + 1, du = 2v dv. So this becomes \( \int \frac{du}{u} = \ln|u| + C_2 = \ln(v^2 + 1) + C_2 \). But since there's a negative sign, the integral becomes \( -\ln(v^2 + 1) + C_2 \) Putting it all together, the integral is: \( \ln|v + 1| - \ln(v^2 + 1) + C \) Combine the logs: \( \ln\left| \frac{v + 1}{v^2 + 1} \right| + C \) So, the left-hand side integral is \( \ln\left| \frac{v + 1}{v^2 + 1} \right| \), and the right-hand side integral is \( \ln|x| + C \). Combining the constants of integration, we can write: \( \ln\left| \frac{v + 1}{v^2 + 1} \right| = \ln|x| + C \) Exponentiating both sides to eliminate the logarithms: \( \left| \frac{v + 1}{v^2 + 1} \right| = C |x| \), where C is now a positive constant. Since we can write the constant as ±C, we can drop the absolute value signs and write: \( \frac{v + 1}{v^2 + 1} = K x \), where K is a constant (can be positive or negative). Now, substitute back \( v = \frac{y}{x} \): \( \frac{\frac{y}{x} + 1}{\left( \frac{y}{x} \right)^2 + 1} = K x \) Simplify numerator and denominator: Numerator: \( \frac{y + x}{x} \) Denominator: \( \frac{y^2 + x^2}{x^2} \) So the entire expression becomes: \( \frac{(y + x)/x}{(y^2 + x^2)/x^2} = \frac{(y + x) x}{y^2 + x^2} = K x \) Simplify both sides: Left side: \( \frac{x(y + x)}{y^2 + x^2} \) Right side: \( K x \) Divide both sides by x (assuming x ≠ 0, which is valid here since the initial condition is at x=1): \( \frac{y + x}{y^2 + x^2} = K \) So the equation simplifies to: \( y + x = K(y^2 + x^2) \) Now, apply the initial condition \( y(1) = -1 \). Substitute x = 1, y = -1 into the equation: Left side: (-1) + 1 = 0 Right side: K((-1)^2 + 1^2) = K(1 + 1) = 2K Therefore, 0 = 2K => K = 0 Wait, but if K = 0, then the equation becomes y + x = 0, so y = -x. Let me check if this satisfies the original differential equation. If y = -x, then dy/dx = -1. Let's compute the right-hand side of the original equation: Numerator: x^2 + 2x(-x) - (-x)^2 = x^2 - 2x^2 - x^2 = -2x^2 Denominator: x^2 - 2x(-x) - (-x)^2 = x^2 + 2x^2 - x^2 = 2x^2 Therefore, RHS = -2x^2 / 2x^2 = -1, which matches dy/dx = -1. So y = -x is indeed a solution. But wait, according to our previous steps, K = 0 gives y + x = 0, which is y = -x. So that's the solution? But let me check: when we exponentiated, we assumed that the constant K is non-zero, but in this case, K turned out to be zero. However, in our partial fractions, we had the absolute values, but since K can be zero, maybe y = -x is the only solution here. Wait, but according to the differential equation, can there be other solutions? Let's see. If we proceed with the general solution: \( y + x = K(y^2 + x^2) \) But with K=0, we get y = -x. Let me check if there are other solutions. Suppose K ≠ 0, then the equation would be y + x = K(y^2 + x^2). But given the initial condition y(1) = -1, substituting x=1, y=-1 gives 0 = K(1 + 1) => K=0. So the only solution satisfying the initial condition is y = -x. Let's verify this. Indeed, if we suppose that the solution is y = -x, then it's a straight line passing through the origin, but with a negative slope. However, when x=1, y=-1, which satisfies the initial condition. Also, the differential equation at any point (x, -x) gives dy/dx = -1, which is consistent. Therefore, y = -x is the solution. Wait, but this seems too straightforward. Let me double-check the steps. Did we lose any solutions during the process? For example, when we divided by something, or when we assumed partial fractions? Let's see. We did partial fractions on the integrand, which is valid as long as the denominator isn't zero. The denominator was (v + 1)(v^2 + 1). Since v^2 + 1 is never zero, and v + 1 = 0 would imply v = -1. But if v = -1, then y = -x. So that's exactly the solution we found. So in this case, y = -x is the only solution passing through (1, -1). So perhaps that's correct. But just to be thorough, let's see if there's a possibility of another solution. Suppose K ≠ 0, but when we plug in x=1, y=-1, we get K=0, so no, there are no other solutions. Therefore, y = -x is the unique solution. But let me think again. When solving the integral, we had: \( \ln\left| \frac{v + 1}{v^2 + 1} \right| = \ln|x| + C \) Exponentiating both sides gives: \( \frac{|v + 1|}{v^2 + 1} = C |x| \), where C is a positive constant. Then we can write: \( \frac{v + 1}{v^2 + 1} = \pm C x = K x \), where K is any real constant (including zero). But in our case, the initial condition forced K to be zero. So even if the general solution is \( \frac{v + 1}{v^2 + 1} = K x \), but K is arbitrary, but with the initial condition x=1, y=-1, so v = y/x = -1/1 = -1. Substituting into the equation: \( \frac{-1 + 1}{(-1)^2 + 1} = \frac{0}{2} = 0 = K * 1 \), so K=0. Therefore, only the solution with K=0 is valid here, leading to y = -x. Hence, the solution to the initial value problem is y = -x. But wait, let's confirm this with the original differential equation. Let's take y = -x. Then dy/dx = -1. Let's compute the right-hand side: Numerator: x^2 + 2x(-x) - (-x)^2 = x^2 - 2x^2 - x^2 = -2x^2 Denominator: x^2 - 2x(-x) - (-x)^2 = x^2 + 2x^2 - x^2 = 2x^2 Thus, RHS = -2x^2 / 2x^2 = -1, which matches dy/dx = -1. Therefore, yes, y = -x is indeed a solution. Since the initial condition leads us to this solution, and the steps didn't involve any loss of generality except when we divided by variables, but since the solution y = -x doesn't violate any of those steps (for x ≠ 0, which is okay around x=1), it's the valid solution. Therefore, the answer is y = -x. To write it in the box, it's \boxed{y = -x}. **Final Answer** \boxed{y = -x} </think> To solve the differential equation \( \frac{dy}{dx} = \frac{x^2 + 2xy - y^2}{x^2 - 2xy - y^2} \) with the initial condition \( y(1) = -1 \), we start by recognizing that it is a homogeneous equation. We use the substitution \( v = \frac{y}{x} \), which transforms the equation into: \[ v + x \frac{dv}{dx} = \frac{1 + 2v - v^2}{1 - 2v - v^2} \] Rearranging and simplifying, we get: \[ x \frac{dv}{dx} = \frac{v^3 + v^2 + v + 1}{1 - 2v - v^2} \] Separating variables, we have: \[ \frac{1 - 2v - v^2}{v^3 + v^2 + v + 1} dv = \frac{1}{x} dx \] Factoring the denominator \( v^3 + v^2 + v + 1 = (v + 1)(v^2 + 1) \) and using partial fraction decomposition, we integrate both sides: \[ \int \left( \frac{1}{v + 1} - \frac{2v}{v^2 + 1} \right) dv = \int \frac{1}{x} dx \] This results in: \[ \ln\left| \frac{v + 1}{v^2 + 1} \right| = \ln|x| + C \] Exponentiating both sides, we get: \[ \frac{v + 1}{v^2 + 1} = Kx \] Substituting back \( v = \frac{y}{x} \), we have: \[ \frac{y + x}{y^2 + x^2} = K \] Applying the initial condition \( y(1) = -1 \), we find \( K = 0 \), leading to the solution \( y = -x \). Verifying this solution, we check that it satisfies both the differential equation and the initial condition. Thus, the solution is: \[ \boxed{y = -x} \]

We use the substitution \( v = \frac{y}{x} \), which transforms the equation into: \[ v + x \frac{dv}{dx} = \frac{1 + 2v - v^2}{1 - 2v - v^2} \] Rearranging and simplifying, we get: \[ x \frac{dv}{dx} = \frac{v^3 + v^2 + v + 1}{1 - 2v - v^2} \] Separating variables, we have: \[ \frac{1 - 2v - v^2}{v^3 + v^2 + v + 1} dv = \frac{1}{x} dx \] Factoring the denominator \( v^3 + v^2 + v + 1 = (v + 1)(v^2 + 1) \) and using partial fraction decomposition, we integrate both sides: \[ \int \left( \frac{1}{v + 1} - \frac{2v}{v^2 + 1} \right) dv = \int \frac{1}{x} dx \] This results in: \[ \ln\left| \frac{v + 1}{v^2 + 1} \right| = \ln|x| + C \] Exponentiating both sides, we get: \[ \frac{v + 1}{v^2 + 1} = Kx \] Substituting back \( v = \frac{y}{x} \), we have: \[ \frac{y + x}{y^2 + x^2} = K \] Applying the initial condition \( y(1) = -1 \), we find \( K = 0 \), leading to the solution \( y = -x \). Verifying this solution, we check that it satisfies both the differential equation and the initial condition. Thus, the solution is: \[ \boxed{y = -x} \]

Problem: Solve the differential equation \( \frac{dy}{dx}=\frac{x^2+2xy-y^2}{x^2-2xy-y^2} \) with the initial condition \( y(1)=-1 \). Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: 0 \boxed{0}

Reference Answer: Okay, so I need to solve this differential equation: \( \frac{dy}{dx} = \frac{x^2 + 2xy - y^2}{x^2 - 2xy - y^2} \) with the initial condition \( y(1) = -1 \). Hmm, let's start by analyzing the equation to see what kind it is and what method might work here. First, both the numerator and the denominator are quadratic in x and y. That makes me think it might be a homogeneous equation. Let me check if that's true. A differential equation is homogeneous if it can be written as \( \frac{dy}{dx} = F\left( \frac{y}{x} \right) \). So let's see if substituting \( v = \frac{y}{x} \) would simplify things. Let me rewrite the equation. The numerator is \( x^2 + 2xy - y^2 \) and the denominator is \( x^2 - 2xy - y^2 \). Let me factor out \( x^2 \) from both numerator and denominator: Numerator: \( x^2 \left( 1 + 2\left( \frac{y}{x} \right) - \left( \frac{y}{x} \right)^2 \right) \) Denominator: \( x^2 \left( 1 - 2\left( \frac{y}{x} \right) - \left( \frac{y}{x} \right)^2 \right) \) So when I divide them, the \( x^2 \) terms cancel out, and I get: \( \frac{dy}{dx} = \frac{1 + 2v - v^2}{1 - 2v - v^2} \), where \( v = \frac{y}{x} \). Alright, so this substitution simplifies the equation. Now, since it's homogeneous, we can use the substitution \( y = vx \), which implies \( \frac{dy}{dx} = v + x \frac{dv}{dx} \). Let me write that down: \( v + x \frac{dv}{dx} = \frac{1 + 2v - v^2}{1 - 2v - v^2} \) So now, I need to solve for \( v \). Let's rearrange the equation to isolate \( \frac{dv}{dx} \): \( x \frac{dv}{dx} = \frac{1 + 2v - v^2}{1 - 2v - v^2} - v \) Let me compute the right-hand side. To subtract these fractions, I need a common denominator: \( \frac{1 + 2v - v^2 - v(1 - 2v - v^2)}{1 - 2v - v^2} \) Expanding the numerator: First term: \( 1 + 2v - v^2 \) Second term: \( -v + 2v^2 + v^3 \) Combine them: 1 + 2v - v^2 - v + 2v^2 + v^3 Combine like terms: 1 + (2v - v) + (-v^2 + 2v^2) + v^3 Which simplifies to: 1 + v + v^2 + v^3 So the equation becomes: \( x \frac{dv}{dx} = \frac{v^3 + v^2 + v + 1}{1 - 2v - v^2} \) Hmm, okay. Let me write that as: \( \frac{dv}{dx} = \frac{v^3 + v^2 + v + 1}{x(1 - 2v - v^2)} \) So this is now a separable equation. I can separate variables by moving all terms involving v to one side and terms involving x to the other side. So: \( \frac{1 - 2v - v^2}{v^3 + v^2 + v + 1} dv = \frac{1}{x} dx \) Now, I need to integrate both sides. The right side is straightforward: \( \int \frac{1}{x} dx = \ln|x| + C \). The left side is more complicated. Let me focus on integrating \( \frac{1 - 2v - v^2}{v^3 + v^2 + v + 1} dv \). First, let's factor the denominator if possible. Let me try to factor \( v^3 + v^2 + v + 1 \). Factor by grouping: \( v^3 + v^2 + v + 1 = v^2(v + 1) + 1(v + 1) = (v^2 + 1)(v + 1) \) Yes! So denominator factors as \( (v + 1)(v^2 + 1) \). That helps. So the integrand becomes: \( \frac{1 - 2v - v^2}{(v + 1)(v^2 + 1)} dv \) Now, let me perform partial fraction decomposition on this rational function. Let's write: \( \frac{1 - 2v - v^2}{(v + 1)(v^2 + 1)} = \frac{A}{v + 1} + \frac{Bv + C}{v^2 + 1} \) Multiply both sides by \( (v + 1)(v^2 + 1) \): \( 1 - 2v - v^2 = A(v^2 + 1) + (Bv + C)(v + 1) \) Let me expand the right-hand side: A(v^2 + 1) + Bv(v + 1) + C(v + 1) = A v^2 + A + B v^2 + B v + C v + C = (A + B) v^2 + (B + C) v + (A + C) Set this equal to the left-hand side: \( -v^2 - 2v + 1 \) So equate coefficients: For \( v^2 \): A + B = -1 For \( v \): B + C = -2 For constant term: A + C = 1 Now, we have a system of equations: 1. A + B = -1 2. B + C = -2 3. A + C = 1 Let me solve this system. Let's subtract equation 3 from equation 1: (A + B) - (A + C) = -1 - 1 => B - C = -2 But from equation 2: B + C = -2 So we have: B - C = -2 B + C = -2 Adding these two equations: 2B = -4 => B = -2 Then from equation 2: -2 + C = -2 => C = 0 From equation 3: A + 0 = 1 => A = 1 So the partial fractions decomposition is: \( \frac{1}{v + 1} + \frac{-2v}{v^2 + 1} \) Therefore, the integral becomes: \( \int \left( \frac{1}{v + 1} - \frac{2v}{v^2 + 1} \right) dv \) Let's integrate term by term: 1. \( \int \frac{1}{v + 1} dv = \ln|v + 1| + C_1 \) 2. \( \int \frac{2v}{v^2 + 1} dv \). Let me substitute u = v^2 + 1, du = 2v dv. So this becomes \( \int \frac{du}{u} = \ln|u| + C_2 = \ln(v^2 + 1) + C_2 \). But since there's a negative sign, the integral becomes \( -\ln(v^2 + 1) + C_2 \) Putting it all together, the integral is: \( \ln|v + 1| - \ln(v^2 + 1) + C \) Combine the logs: \( \ln\left| \frac{v + 1}{v^2 + 1} \right| + C \) So, the left-hand side integral is \( \ln\left| \frac{v + 1}{v^2 + 1} \right| \), and the right-hand side integral is \( \ln|x| + C \). Combining the constants of integration, we can write: \( \ln\left| \frac{v + 1}{v^2 + 1} \right| = \ln|x| + C \) Exponentiating both sides to eliminate the logarithms: \( \left| \frac{v + 1}{v^2 + 1} \right| = C |x| \), where C is now a positive constant. Since we can write the constant as ±C, we can drop the absolute value signs and write: \( \frac{v + 1}{v^2 + 1} = K x \), where K is a constant (can be positive or negative). Now, substitute back \( v = \frac{y}{x} \): \( \frac{\frac{y}{x} + 1}{\left( \frac{y}{x} \right)^2 + 1} = K x \) Simplify numerator and denominator: Numerator: \( \frac{y + x}{x} \) Denominator: \( \frac{y^2 + x^2}{x^2} \) So the entire expression becomes: \( \frac{(y + x)/x}{(y^2 + x^2)/x^2} = \frac{(y + x) x}{y^2 + x^2} = K x \) Simplify both sides: Left side: \( \frac{x(y + x)}{y^2 + x^2} \) Right side: \( K x \) Divide both sides by x (assuming x ≠ 0, which is valid here since the initial condition is at x=1): \( \frac{y + x}{y^2 + x^2} = K \) So the equation simplifies to: \( y + x = K(y^2 + x^2) \) Now, apply the initial condition \( y(1) = -1 \). Substitute x = 1, y = -1 into the equation: Left side: (-1) + 1 = 0 Right side: K((-1)^2 + 1^2) = K(1 + 1) = 2K Therefore, 0 = 2K => K = 0 Wait, but if K = 0, then the equation becomes y + x = 0, so y = -x. Let me check if this satisfies the original differential equation. If y = -x, then dy/dx = -1. So y = -x is indeed a solution. But wait, according to our previous steps, K = 0 gives y + x = 0, which is y = -x. So that's the solution? Let's compute the right-hand side: Numerator: x^2 + 2x(-x) - (-x)^2 = x^2 - 2x^2 - x^2 = -2x^2 Denominator: x^2 - 2x(-x) - (-x)^2 = x^2 + 2x^2 - x^2 = 2x^2 Thus, RHS = -2x^2 / 2x^2 = -1, which matches dy/dx = -1. Therefore, yes, y = -x is indeed a solution. Since the initial condition leads us to this solution, and the steps didn't involve any loss of generality except when we divided by variables, but since the solution y = -x doesn't violate any of those steps (for x ≠ 0, which is okay around x=1), it's the valid solution. Therefore, the answer is y = -x. To write it in the box, it's \boxed{y = -x}. **Final Answer** \boxed{y = -x} </think> To solve the differential equation \( \frac{dy}{dx} = \frac{x^2 + 2xy - y^2}{x^2 - 2xy - y^2} \) with the initial condition \( y(1) = -1 \), we start by recognizing that it is a homogeneous equation. We use the substitution \( v = \frac{y}{x} \), which transforms the equation into: \[ v + x \frac{dv}{dx} = \frac{1 + 2v - v^2}{1 - 2v - v^2} \] Rearranging and simplifying, we get: \[ x \frac{dv}{dx} = \frac{v^3 + v^2 + v + 1}{1 - 2v - v^2} \] Separating variables, we have: \[ \frac{1 - 2v - v^2}{v^3 + v^2 + v + 1} dv = \frac{1}{x} dx \] Factoring the denominator \( v^3 + v^2 + v + 1 = (v + 1)(v^2 + 1) \) and using partial fraction decomposition, we integrate both sides: \[ \int \left( \frac{1}{v + 1} - \frac{2v}{v^2 + 1} \right) dv = \int \frac{1}{x} dx \] This results in: \[ \ln\left| \frac{v + 1}{v^2 + 1} \right| = \ln|x| + C \] Exponentiating both sides, we get: \[ \frac{v + 1}{v^2 + 1} = Kx \] Substituting back \( v = \frac{y}{x} \), we have: \[ \frac{y + x}{y^2 + x^2} = K \] Applying the initial condition \( y(1) = -1 \), we find \( K = 0 \), leading to the solution \( y = -x \). Verifying this solution, we check that it satisfies both the differential equation and the initial condition. Thus, the solution is: \[ \boxed{y = -x} \]

Problem: Solve the differential equation \( \frac{dy}{dx}=\frac{x^2+2xy-y^2}{x^2-2xy-y^2} \) with the initial condition \( y(1)=-1 \). Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: \boxed{0}

Reference Answer: Okay, so I need to solve this differential equation: \( \frac{dy}{dx} = \frac{x^2 + 2xy - y^2}{x^2 - 2xy - y^2} \) with the initial condition \( y(1) = -1 \). Hmm, let's start by analyzing the equation to see what kind it is and what method might work here. First, both the numerator and the denominator are quadratic in x and y. That makes me think it might be a homogeneous equation. Let me check if that's true. A differential equation is homogeneous if it can be written as \( \frac{dy}{dx} = F\left( \frac{y}{x} \right) \). So let's see if substituting \( v = \frac{y}{x} \) would simplify things. Let me rewrite the equation. The numerator is \( x^2 + 2xy - y^2 \) and the denominator is \( x^2 - 2xy - y^2 \). Let me factor out \( x^2 \) from both numerator and denominator: Numerator: \( x^2 \left( 1 + 2\left( \frac{y}{x} \right) - \left( \frac{y}{x} \right)^2 \right) \) Denominator: \( x^2 \left( 1 - 2\left( \frac{y}{x} \right) - \left( \frac{y}{x} \right)^2 \right) \) So when I divide them, the \( x^2 \) terms cancel out, and I get: \( \frac{dy}{dx} = \frac{1 + 2v - v^2}{1 - 2v - v^2} \), where \( v = \frac{y}{x} \). Alright, so this substitution simplifies the equation. Now, since it's homogeneous, we can use the substitution \( y = vx \), which implies \( \frac{dy}{dx} = v + x \frac{dv}{dx} \). Let me write that down: \( v + x \frac{dv}{dx} = \frac{1 + 2v - v^2}{1 - 2v - v^2} \) So now, I need to solve for \( v \). Let's rearrange the equation to isolate \( \frac{dv}{dx} \): \( x \frac{dv}{dx} = \frac{1 + 2v - v^2}{1 - 2v - v^2} - v \) Let me compute the right-hand side. To subtract these fractions, I need a common denominator: \( \frac{1 + 2v - v^2 - v(1 - 2v - v^2)}{1 - 2v - v^2} \) Expanding the numerator: First term: \( 1 + 2v - v^2 \) Second term: \( -v + 2v^2 + v^3 \) Combine them: 1 + 2v - v^2 - v + 2v^2 + v^3 Combine like terms: 1 + (2v - v) + (-v^2 + 2v^2) + v^3 Which simplifies to: 1 + v + v^2 + v^3 So the equation becomes: \( x \frac{dv}{dx} = \frac{v^3 + v^2 + v + 1}{1 - 2v - v^2} \) Hmm, okay. Let me write that as: \( \frac{dv}{dx} = \frac{v^3 + v^2 + v + 1}{x(1 - 2v - v^2)} \) So this is now a separable equation. I can separate variables by moving all terms involving v to one side and terms involving x to the other side. So: \( \frac{1 - 2v - v^2}{v^3 + v^2 + v + 1} dv = \frac{1}{x} dx \) Now, I need to integrate both sides. The right side is straightforward: \( \int \frac{1}{x} dx = \ln|x| + C \). The left side is more complicated. Let me focus on integrating \( \frac{1 - 2v - v^2}{v^3 + v^2 + v + 1} dv \). First, let's factor the denominator if possible. Let me try to factor \( v^3 + v^2 + v + 1 \). Factor by grouping: \( v^3 + v^2 + v + 1 = v^2(v + 1) + 1(v + 1) = (v^2 + 1)(v + 1) \) Yes! So denominator factors as \( (v + 1)(v^2 + 1) \). That helps. So the integrand becomes: \( \frac{1 - 2v - v^2}{(v + 1)(v^2 + 1)} dv \) Now, let me perform partial fraction decomposition on this rational function. Let's write: \( \frac{1 - 2v - v^2}{(v + 1)(v^2 + 1)} = \frac{A}{v + 1} + \frac{Bv + C}{v^2 + 1} \) Multiply both sides by \( (v + 1)(v^2 + 1) \): \( 1 - 2v - v^2 = A(v^2 + 1) + (Bv + C)(v + 1) \) Let me expand the right-hand side: A(v^2 + 1) + Bv(v + 1) + C(v + 1) = A v^2 + A + B v^2 + B v + C v + C = (A + B) v^2 + (B + C) v + (A + C) Set this equal to the left-hand side: \( -v^2 - 2v + 1 \) So equate coefficients: For \( v^2 \): A + B = -1 For \( v \): B + C = -2 For constant term: A + C = 1 Now, we have a system of equations: 1. A + B = -1 2. B + C = -2 3. A + C = 1 Let me solve this system. Let's subtract equation 3 from equation 1: (A + B) - (A + C) = -1 - 1 => B - C = -2 But from equation 2: B + C = -2 So we have: B - C = -2 B + C = -2 Adding these two equations: 2B = -4 => B = -2 Then from equation 2: -2 + C = -2 => C = 0 From equation 3: A + 0 = 1 => A = 1 So the partial fractions decomposition is: \( \frac{1}{v + 1} + \frac{-2v}{v^2 + 1} \) Therefore, the integral becomes: \( \int \left( \frac{1}{v + 1} - \frac{2v}{v^2 + 1} \right) dv \) Let's integrate term by term: 1. \( \int \frac{1}{v + 1} dv = \ln|v + 1| + C_1 \) 2. \( \int \frac{2v}{v^2 + 1} dv \). Let me substitute u = v^2 + 1, du = 2v dv. So this becomes \( \int \frac{du}{u} = \ln|u| + C_2 = \ln(v^2 + 1) + C_2 \). But since there's a negative sign, the integral becomes \( -\ln(v^2 + 1) + C_2 \) Putting it all together, the integral is: \( \ln|v + 1| - \ln(v^2 + 1) + C \) Combine the logs: \( \ln\left| \frac{v + 1}{v^2 + 1} \right| + C \) So, the left-hand side integral is \( \ln\left| \frac{v + 1}{v^2 + 1} \right| \), and the right-hand side integral is \( \ln|x| + C \). Combining the constants of integration, we can write: \( \ln\left| \frac{v + 1}{v^2 + 1} \right| = \ln|x| + C \) Exponentiating both sides to eliminate the logarithms: \( \left| \frac{v + 1}{v^2 + 1} \right| = C |x| \), where C is now a positive constant. Since we can write the constant as ±C, we can drop the absolute value signs and write: \( \frac{v + 1}{v^2 + 1} = K x \), where K is a constant (can be positive or negative). Now, substitute back \( v = \frac{y}{x} \): \( \frac{\frac{y}{x} + 1}{\left( \frac{y}{x} \right)^2 + 1} = K x \) Simplify numerator and denominator: Numerator: \( \frac{y + x}{x} \) Denominator: \( \frac{y^2 + x^2}{x^2} \) So the entire expression becomes: \( \frac{(y + x)/x}{(y^2 + x^2)/x^2} = \frac{(y + x) x}{y^2 + x^2} = K x \) Simplify both sides: Left side: \( \frac{x(y + x)}{y^2 + x^2} \) Right side: \( K x \) Divide both sides by x (assuming x ≠ 0, which is valid here since the initial condition is at x=1): \( \frac{y + x}{y^2 + x^2} = K \) So the equation simplifies to: \( y + x = K(y^2 + x^2) \) Now, apply the initial condition \( y(1) = -1 \). When solving the integral, we had: \( \ln\left| \frac{v + 1}{v^2 + 1} \right| = \ln|x| + C \) Exponentiating both sides gives: \( \frac{|v + 1|}{v^2 + 1} = C |x| \), where C is a positive constant. Then we can write: \( \frac{v + 1}{v^2 + 1} = \pm C x = K x \), where K is any real constant (including zero). But in our case, the initial condition forced K to be zero. So even if the general solution is \( \frac{v + 1}{v^2 + 1} = K x \), but K is arbitrary, but with the initial condition x=1, y=-1, so v = y/x = -1/1 = -1. Substituting into the equation: \( \frac{-1 + 1}{(-1)^2 + 1} = \frac{0}{2} = 0 = K * 1 \), so K=0. Therefore, only the solution with K=0 is valid here, leading to y = -x. Hence, the solution to the initial value problem is y = -x. But wait, let's confirm this with the original differential equation. Let's take y = -x. Then dy/dx = -1. Let's compute the right-hand side: Numerator: x^2 + 2x(-x) - (-x)^2 = x^2 - 2x^2 - x^2 = -2x^2 Denominator: x^2 - 2x(-x) - (-x)^2 = x^2 + 2x^2 - x^2 = 2x^2 Thus, RHS = -2x^2 / 2x^2 = -1, which matches dy/dx = -1. Therefore, yes, y = -x is indeed a solution. Since the initial condition leads us to this solution, and the steps didn't involve any loss of generality except when we divided by variables, but since the solution y = -x doesn't violate any of those steps (for x ≠ 0, which is okay around x=1), it's the valid solution. Therefore, the answer is y = -x. To write it in the box, it's \boxed{y = -x}. **Final Answer** \boxed{y = -x} </think> To solve the differential equation \( \frac{dy}{dx} = \frac{x^2 + 2xy - y^2}{x^2 - 2xy - y^2} \) with the initial condition \( y(1) = -1 \), we start by recognizing that it is a homogeneous equation. We use the substitution \( v = \frac{y}{x} \), which transforms the equation into: \[ v + x \frac{dv}{dx} = \frac{1 + 2v - v^2}{1 - 2v - v^2} \] Rearranging and simplifying, we get: \[ x \frac{dv}{dx} = \frac{v^3 + v^2 + v + 1}{1 - 2v - v^2} \] Separating variables, we have: \[ \frac{1 - 2v - v^2}{v^3 + v^2 + v + 1} dv = \frac{1}{x} dx \] Factoring the denominator \( v^3 + v^2 + v + 1 = (v + 1)(v^2 + 1) \) and using partial fraction decomposition, we integrate both sides: \[ \int \left( \frac{1}{v + 1} - \frac{2v}{v^2 + 1} \right) dv = \int \frac{1}{x} dx \] This results in: \[ \ln\left| \frac{v + 1}{v^2 + 1} \right| = \ln|x| + C \] Exponentiating both sides, we get: \[ \frac{v + 1}{v^2 + 1} = Kx \] Substituting back \( v = \frac{y}{x} \), we have: \[ \frac{y + x}{y^2 + x^2} = K \] Applying the initial condition \( y(1) = -1 \), we find \( K = 0 \), leading to the solution \( y = -x \). Verifying this solution, we check that it satisfies both the differential equation and the initial condition. Thus, the solution is: \[ \boxed{y = -x} \]

Problem: Solve the differential equation \( \frac{dy}{dx}=\frac{x^2+2xy-y^2}{x^2-2xy-y^2} \) with the initial condition \( y(1)=-1 \). Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Reference Answer: Okay, so I need to solve this differential equation: \( \frac{dy}{dx} = \frac{x^2 + 2xy - y^2}{x^2 - 2xy - y^2} \) with the initial condition \( y(1) = -1 \). Hmm, let's start by analyzing the equation to see what kind it is and what method might work here. First, both the numerator and the denominator are quadratic in x and y. That makes me think it might be a homogeneous equation. Let me check if that's true. A differential equation is homogeneous if it can be written as \( \frac{dy}{dx} = F\left( \frac{y}{x} \right) \). So let's see if substituting \( v = \frac{y}{x} \) would simplify things. Let me rewrite the equation. The numerator is \( x^2 + 2xy - y^2 \) and the denominator is \( x^2 - 2xy - y^2 \). Let me factor out \( x^2 \) from both numerator and denominator: Numerator: \( x^2 \left( 1 + 2\left( \frac{y}{x} \right) - \left( \frac{y}{x} \right)^2 \right) \) Denominator: \( x^2 \left( 1 - 2\left( \frac{y}{x} \right) - \left( \frac{y}{x} \right)^2 \right) \) So when I divide them, the \( x^2 \) terms cancel out, and I get: \( \frac{dy}{dx} = \frac{1 + 2v - v^2}{1 - 2v - v^2} \), where \( v = \frac{y}{x} \). Alright, so this substitution simplifies the equation. Now, since it's homogeneous, we can use the substitution \( y = vx \), which implies \( \frac{dy}{dx} = v + x \frac{dv}{dx} \). Let me write that down: \( v + x \frac{dv}{dx} = \frac{1 + 2v - v^2}{1 - 2v - v^2} \) So now, I need to solve for \( v \). Let's rearrange the equation to isolate \( \frac{dv}{dx} \): \( x \frac{dv}{dx} = \frac{1 + 2v - v^2}{1 - 2v - v^2} - v \) Let me compute the right-hand side. To subtract these fractions, I need a common denominator: \( \frac{1 + 2v - v^2 - v(1 - 2v - v^2)}{1 - 2v - v^2} \) Expanding the numerator: First term: \( 1 + 2v - v^2 \) Second term: \( -v + 2v^2 + v^3 \) Combine them: 1 + 2v - v^2 - v + 2v^2 + v^3 Combine like terms: 1 + (2v - v) + (-v^2 + 2v^2) + v^3 Which simplifies to: 1 + v + v^2 + v^3 So the equation becomes: \( x \frac{dv}{dx} = \frac{v^3 + v^2 + v + 1}{1 - 2v - v^2} \) Hmm, okay. Let me write that as: \( \frac{dv}{dx} = \frac{v^3 + v^2 + v + 1}{x(1 - 2v - v^2)} \) So this is now a separable equation. I can separate variables by moving all terms involving v to one side and terms involving x to the other side. So: \( \frac{1 - 2v - v^2}{v^3 + v^2 + v + 1} dv = \frac{1}{x} dx \) Now, I need to integrate both sides. The right side is straightforward: \( \int \frac{1}{x} dx = \ln|x| + C \). The left side is more complicated. Let me focus on integrating \( \frac{1 - 2v - v^2}{v^3 + v^2 + v + 1} dv \). First, let's factor the denominator if possible. Let me try to factor \( v^3 + v^2 + v + 1 \). Factor by grouping: \( v^3 + v^2 + v + 1 = v^2(v + 1) + 1(v + 1) = (v^2 + 1)(v + 1) \) Yes! So denominator factors as \( (v + 1)(v^2 + 1) \). That helps. So the integrand becomes: \( \frac{1 - 2v - v^2}{(v + 1)(v^2 + 1)} dv \) Now, let me perform partial fraction decomposition on this rational function. Let's write: \( \frac{1 - 2v - v^2}{(v + 1)(v^2 + 1)} = \frac{A}{v + 1} + \frac{Bv + C}{v^2 + 1} \) Multiply both sides by \( (v + 1)(v^2 + 1) \): \( 1 - 2v - v^2 = A(v^2 + 1) + (Bv + C)(v + 1) \) Let me expand the right-hand side: A(v^2 + 1) + Bv(v + 1) + C(v + 1) = A v^2 + A + B v^2 + B v + C v + C = (A + B) v^2 + (B + C) v + (A + C) Set this equal to the left-hand side: \( -v^2 - 2v + 1 \) So equate coefficients: For \( v^2 \): A + B = -1 For \( v \): B + C = -2 For constant term: A + C = 1 Now, we have a system of equations: 1. A + B = -1 2. B + C = -2 3. A + C = 1 Let me solve this system. Let's subtract equation 3 from equation 1: (A + B) - (A + C) = -1 - 1 => B - C = -2 But from equation 2: B + C = -2 So we have: B - C = -2 B + C = -2 Adding these two equations: 2B = -4 => B = -2 Then from equation 2: -2 + C = -2 => C = 0 From equation 3: A + 0 = 1 => A = 1 So the partial fractions decomposition is: \( \frac{1}{v + 1} + \frac{-2v}{v^2 + 1} \) Therefore, the integral becomes: \( \int \left( \frac{1}{v + 1} - \frac{2v}{v^2 + 1} \right) dv \) Let's integrate term by term: 1. \( \int \frac{1}{v + 1} dv = \ln|v + 1| + C_1 \) 2. \( \int \frac{2v}{v^2 + 1} dv \). Let me substitute u = v^2 + 1, du = 2v dv. So this becomes \( \int \frac{du}{u} = \ln|u| + C_2 = \ln(v^2 + 1) + C_2 \). Combining the constants of integration, we can write: \( \ln\left| \frac{v + 1}{v^2 + 1} \right| = \ln|x| + C \) Exponentiating both sides to eliminate the logarithms: \( \left| \frac{v + 1}{v^2 + 1} \right| = C |x| \), where C is now a positive constant. Since we can write the constant as ±C, we can drop the absolute value signs and write: \( \frac{v + 1}{v^2 + 1} = K x \), where K is a constant (can be positive or negative). Also, the differential equation at any point (x, -x) gives dy/dx = -1, which is consistent. Therefore, y = -x is the solution. Wait, but this seems too straightforward. Let me double-check the steps. Did we lose any solutions during the process? For example, when we divided by something, or when we assumed partial fractions? Let's see. We did partial fractions on the integrand, which is valid as long as the denominator isn't zero. The denominator was (v + 1)(v^2 + 1). Since v^2 + 1 is never zero, and v + 1 = 0 would imply v = -1. But if v = -1, then y = -x. So that's exactly the solution we found. So in this case, y = -x is the only solution passing through (1, -1). So perhaps that's correct. But just to be thorough, let's see if there's a possibility of another solution. Suppose K ≠ 0, but when we plug in x=1, y=-1, we get K=0, so no, there are no other solutions. Therefore, y = -x is the unique solution. But let me think again. When solving the integral, we had: \( \ln\left| \frac{v + 1}{v^2 + 1} \right| = \ln|x| + C \) Exponentiating both sides gives: \( \frac{|v + 1|}{v^2 + 1} = C |x| \), where C is a positive constant. Then we can write: \( \frac{v + 1}{v^2 + 1} = \pm C x = K x \), where K is any real constant (including zero). But in our case, the initial condition forced K to be zero. So even if the general solution is \( \frac{v + 1}{v^2 + 1} = K x \), but K is arbitrary, but with the initial condition x=1, y=-1, so v = y/x = -1/1 = -1. Substituting into the equation: \( \frac{-1 + 1}{(-1)^2 + 1} = \frac{0}{2} = 0 = K * 1 \), so K=0. Therefore, only the solution with K=0 is valid here, leading to y = -x. Hence, the solution to the initial value problem is y = -x. But wait, let's confirm this with the original differential equation. Let's take y = -x. Then dy/dx = -1. Let's compute the right-hand side: Numerator: x^2 + 2x(-x) - (-x)^2 = x^2 - 2x^2 - x^2 = -2x^2 Denominator: x^2 - 2x(-x) - (-x)^2 = x^2 + 2x^2 - x^2 = 2x^2 Thus, RHS = -2x^2 / 2x^2 = -1, which matches dy/dx = -1. Therefore, yes, y = -x is indeed a solution. Since the initial condition leads us to this solution, and the steps didn't involve any loss of generality except when we divided by variables, but since the solution y = -x doesn't violate any of those steps (for x ≠ 0, which is okay around x=1), it's the valid solution. Therefore, the answer is y = -x. To write it in the box, it's \boxed{y = -x}. **Final Answer** \boxed{y = -x} </think> To solve the differential equation \( \frac{dy}{dx} = \frac{x^2 + 2xy - y^2}{x^2 - 2xy - y^2} \) with the initial condition \( y(1) = -1 \), we start by recognizing that it is a homogeneous equation. We use the substitution \( v = \frac{y}{x} \), which transforms the equation into: \[ v + x \frac{dv}{dx} = \frac{1 + 2v - v^2}{1 - 2v - v^2} \] Rearranging and simplifying, we get: \[ x \frac{dv}{dx} = \frac{v^3 + v^2 + v + 1}{1 - 2v - v^2} \] Separating variables, we have: \[ \frac{1 - 2v - v^2}{v^3 + v^2 + v + 1} dv = \frac{1}{x} dx \] Factoring the denominator \( v^3 + v^2 + v + 1 = (v + 1)(v^2 + 1) \) and using partial fraction decomposition, we integrate both sides: \[ \int \left( \frac{1}{v + 1} - \frac{2v}{v^2 + 1} \right) dv = \int \frac{1}{x} dx \] This results in: \[ \ln\left| \frac{v + 1}{v^2 + 1} \right| = \ln|x| + C \] Exponentiating both sides, we get: \[ \frac{v + 1}{v^2 + 1} = Kx \] Substituting back \( v = \frac{y}{x} \), we have: \[ \frac{y + x}{y^2 + x^2} = K \] Applying the initial condition \( y(1) = -1 \), we find \( K = 0 \), leading to the solution \( y = -x \). Verifying this solution, we check that it satisfies both the differential equation and the initial condition. Thus, the solution is: \[ \boxed{y = -x} \]

Problem: Solve the differential equation \( \frac{dy}{dx}=\frac{x^2+2xy-y^2}{x^2-2xy-y^2} \) with the initial condition \( y(1)=-1 \). Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: \boxed{-\frac{y}{x}+\frac{1}{x}=0}

Reference Answer: Okay, so I need to solve this differential equation: \( \frac{dy}{dx} = \frac{x^2 + 2xy - y^2}{x^2 - 2xy - y^2} \) with the initial condition \( y(1) = -1 \). Hmm, let's start by analyzing the equation to see what kind it is and what method might work here. First, both the numerator and the denominator are quadratic in x and y. That makes me think it might be a homogeneous equation. Let me check if that's true. A differential equation is homogeneous if it can be written as \( \frac{dy}{dx} = F\left( \frac{y}{x} \right) \). So let's see if substituting \( v = \frac{y}{x} \) would simplify things. Let me rewrite the equation. The numerator is \( x^2 + 2xy - y^2 \) and the denominator is \( x^2 - 2xy - y^2 \). Let me factor out \( x^2 \) from both numerator and denominator: Numerator: \( x^2 \left( 1 + 2\left( \frac{y}{x} \right) - \left( \frac{y}{x} \right)^2 \right) \) Denominator: \( x^2 \left( 1 - 2\left( \frac{y}{x} \right) - \left( \frac{y}{x} \right)^2 \right) \) So when I divide them, the \( x^2 \) terms cancel out, and I get: \( \frac{dy}{dx} = \frac{1 + 2v - v^2}{1 - 2v - v^2} \), where \( v = \frac{y}{x} \). Alright, so this substitution simplifies the equation. Now, since it's homogeneous, we can use the substitution \( y = vx \), which implies \( \frac{dy}{dx} = v + x \frac{dv}{dx} \). Let me write that down: \( v + x \frac{dv}{dx} = \frac{1 + 2v - v^2}{1 - 2v - v^2} \) So now, I need to solve for \( v \). Let's rearrange the equation to isolate \( \frac{dv}{dx} \): \( x \frac{dv}{dx} = \frac{1 + 2v - v^2}{1 - 2v - v^2} - v \) Let me compute the right-hand side. To subtract these fractions, I need a common denominator: \( \frac{1 + 2v - v^2 - v(1 - 2v - v^2)}{1 - 2v - v^2} \) Expanding the numerator: First term: \( 1 + 2v - v^2 \) Second term: \( -v + 2v^2 + v^3 \) Combine them: 1 + 2v - v^2 - v + 2v^2 + v^3 Combine like terms: 1 + (2v - v) + (-v^2 + 2v^2) + v^3 Which simplifies to: 1 + v + v^2 + v^3 So the equation becomes: \( x \frac{dv}{dx} = \frac{v^3 + v^2 + v + 1}{1 - 2v - v^2} \) Hmm, okay. Let me write that as: \( \frac{dv}{dx} = \frac{v^3 + v^2 + v + 1}{x(1 - 2v - v^2)} \) So this is now a separable equation. I can separate variables by moving all terms involving v to one side and terms involving x to the other side. So: \( \frac{1 - 2v - v^2}{v^3 + v^2 + v + 1} dv = \frac{1}{x} dx \) Now, I need to integrate both sides. The right side is straightforward: \( \int \frac{1}{x} dx = \ln|x| + C \). The left side is more complicated. Let me focus on integrating \( \frac{1 - 2v - v^2}{v^3 + v^2 + v + 1} dv \). First, let's factor the denominator if possible. Let me try to factor \( v^3 + v^2 + v + 1 \). Factor by grouping: \( v^3 + v^2 + v + 1 = v^2(v + 1) + 1(v + 1) = (v^2 + 1)(v + 1) \) Yes! So denominator factors as \( (v + 1)(v^2 + 1) \). That helps. So the integrand becomes: \( \frac{1 - 2v - v^2}{(v + 1)(v^2 + 1)} dv \) Now, let me perform partial fraction decomposition on this rational function. Let's write: \( \frac{1 - 2v - v^2}{(v + 1)(v^2 + 1)} = \frac{A}{v + 1} + \frac{Bv + C}{v^2 + 1} \) Multiply both sides by \( (v + 1)(v^2 + 1) \): \( 1 - 2v - v^2 = A(v^2 + 1) + (Bv + C)(v + 1) \) Let me expand the right-hand side: A(v^2 + 1) + Bv(v + 1) + C(v + 1) = A v^2 + A + B v^2 + B v + C v + C = (A + B) v^2 + (B + C) v + (A + C) Set this equal to the left-hand side: \( -v^2 - 2v + 1 \) So equate coefficients: For \( v^2 \): A + B = -1 For \( v \): B + C = -2 For constant term: A + C = 1 Now, we have a system of equations: 1. A + B = -1 2. B + C = -2 3. A + C = 1 Let me solve this system. Let's subtract equation 3 from equation 1: (A + B) - (A + C) = -1 - 1 => B - C = -2 But from equation 2: B + C = -2 So we have: B - C = -2 B + C = -2 Adding these two equations: 2B = -4 => B = -2 Then from equation 2: -2 + C = -2 => C = 0 From equation 3: A + 0 = 1 => A = 1 So the partial fractions decomposition is: \( \frac{1}{v + 1} + \frac{-2v}{v^2 + 1} \) Therefore, the integral becomes: \( \int \left( \frac{1}{v + 1} - \frac{2v}{v^2 + 1} \right) dv \) Let's integrate term by term: 1. \( \int \frac{1}{v + 1} dv = \ln|v + 1| + C_1 \) 2. \( \int \frac{2v}{v^2 + 1} dv \). Let me substitute u = v^2 + 1, du = 2v dv. So this becomes \( \int \frac{du}{u} = \ln|u| + C_2 = \ln(v^2 + 1) + C_2 \). But since there's a negative sign, the integral becomes \( -\ln(v^2 + 1) + C_2 \) Putting it all together, the integral is: \( \ln|v + 1| - \ln(v^2 + 1) + C \) Combine the logs: \( \ln\left| \frac{v + 1}{v^2 + 1} \right| + C \) So, the left-hand side integral is \( \ln\left| \frac{v + 1}{v^2 + 1} \right| \), and the right-hand side integral is \( \ln|x| + C \). Combining the constants of integration, we can write: \( \ln\left| \frac{v + 1}{v^2 + 1} \right| = \ln|x| + C \) Exponentiating both sides to eliminate the logarithms: \( \left| \frac{v + 1}{v^2 + 1} \right| = C |x| \), where C is now a positive constant. Since we can write the constant as ±C, we can drop the absolute value signs and write: \( \frac{v + 1}{v^2 + 1} = K x \), where K is a constant (can be positive or negative). Now, substitute back \( v = \frac{y}{x} \): \( \frac{\frac{y}{x} + 1}{\left( \frac{y}{x} \right)^2 + 1} = K x \) Simplify numerator and denominator: Numerator: \( \frac{y + x}{x} \) Denominator: \( \frac{y^2 + x^2}{x^2} \) So the entire expression becomes: \( \frac{(y + x)/x}{(y^2 + x^2)/x^2} = \frac{(y + x) x}{y^2 + x^2} = K x \) Simplify both sides: Left side: \( \frac{x(y + x)}{y^2 + x^2} \) Right side: \( K x \) Divide both sides by x (assuming x ≠ 0, which is valid here since the initial condition is at x=1): \( \frac{y + x}{y^2 + x^2} = K \) So the equation simplifies to: \( y + x = K(y^2 + x^2) \) Now, apply the initial condition \( y(1) = -1 \). Substitute x = 1, y = -1 into the equation: Left side: (-1) + 1 = 0 Right side: K((-1)^2 + 1^2) = K(1 + 1) = 2K Therefore, 0 = 2K => K = 0 Wait, but if K = 0, then the equation becomes y + x = 0, so y = -x. Let me check if this satisfies the original differential equation. If y = -x, then dy/dx = -1. Let's compute the right-hand side of the original equation: Numerator: x^2 + 2x(-x) - (-x)^2 = x^2 - 2x^2 - x^2 = -2x^2 Denominator: x^2 - 2x(-x) - (-x)^2 = x^2 + 2x^2 - x^2 = 2x^2 Therefore, RHS = -2x^2 / 2x^2 = -1, which matches dy/dx = -1. So y = -x is indeed a solution. But wait, according to our previous steps, K = 0 gives y + x = 0, which is y = -x. So that's the solution? We use the substitution \( v = \frac{y}{x} \), which transforms the equation into: \[ v + x \frac{dv}{dx} = \frac{1 + 2v - v^2}{1 - 2v - v^2} \] Rearranging and simplifying, we get: \[ x \frac{dv}{dx} = \frac{v^3 + v^2 + v + 1}{1 - 2v - v^2} \] Separating variables, we have: \[ \frac{1 - 2v - v^2}{v^3 + v^2 + v + 1} dv = \frac{1}{x} dx \] Factoring the denominator \( v^3 + v^2 + v + 1 = (v + 1)(v^2 + 1) \) and using partial fraction decomposition, we integrate both sides: \[ \int \left( \frac{1}{v + 1} - \frac{2v}{v^2 + 1} \right) dv = \int \frac{1}{x} dx \] This results in: \[ \ln\left| \frac{v + 1}{v^2 + 1} \right| = \ln|x| + C \] Exponentiating both sides, we get: \[ \frac{v + 1}{v^2 + 1} = Kx \] Substituting back \( v = \frac{y}{x} \), we have: \[ \frac{y + x}{y^2 + x^2} = K \] Applying the initial condition \( y(1) = -1 \), we find \( K = 0 \), leading to the solution \( y = -x \). Verifying this solution, we check that it satisfies both the differential equation and the initial condition. Thus, the solution is: \[ \boxed{y = -x} \]

Problem: Solve the differential equation \( \frac{dy}{dx}=\frac{x^2+2xy-y^2}{x^2-2xy-y^2} \) with the initial condition \( y(1)=-1 \). Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: \boxed{y=x}

Reference Answer: Okay, so I need to solve this differential equation: \( \frac{dy}{dx} = \frac{x^2 + 2xy - y^2}{x^2 - 2xy - y^2} \) with the initial condition \( y(1) = -1 \). Hmm, let's start by analyzing the equation to see what kind it is and what method might work here. First, both the numerator and the denominator are quadratic in x and y. That makes me think it might be a homogeneous equation. Let me check if that's true. A differential equation is homogeneous if it can be written as \( \frac{dy}{dx} = F\left( \frac{y}{x} \right) \). So let's see if substituting \( v = \frac{y}{x} \) would simplify things. Let me rewrite the equation. The numerator is \( x^2 + 2xy - y^2 \) and the denominator is \( x^2 - 2xy - y^2 \). Let me factor out \( x^2 \) from both numerator and denominator: Numerator: \( x^2 \left( 1 + 2\left( \frac{y}{x} \right) - \left( \frac{y}{x} \right)^2 \right) \) Denominator: \( x^2 \left( 1 - 2\left( \frac{y}{x} \right) - \left( \frac{y}{x} \right)^2 \right) \) So when I divide them, the \( x^2 \) terms cancel out, and I get: \( \frac{dy}{dx} = \frac{1 + 2v - v^2}{1 - 2v - v^2} \), where \( v = \frac{y}{x} \). Alright, so this substitution simplifies the equation. Now, since it's homogeneous, we can use the substitution \( y = vx \), which implies \( \frac{dy}{dx} = v + x \frac{dv}{dx} \). Let me write that down: \( v + x \frac{dv}{dx} = \frac{1 + 2v - v^2}{1 - 2v - v^2} \) So now, I need to solve for \( v \). Let's rearrange the equation to isolate \( \frac{dv}{dx} \): \( x \frac{dv}{dx} = \frac{1 + 2v - v^2}{1 - 2v - v^2} - v \) Let me compute the right-hand side. To subtract these fractions, I need a common denominator: \( \frac{1 + 2v - v^2 - v(1 - 2v - v^2)}{1 - 2v - v^2} \) Expanding the numerator: First term: \( 1 + 2v - v^2 \) Second term: \( -v + 2v^2 + v^3 \) Combine them: 1 + 2v - v^2 - v + 2v^2 + v^3 Combine like terms: 1 + (2v - v) + (-v^2 + 2v^2) + v^3 Which simplifies to: 1 + v + v^2 + v^3 So the equation becomes: \( x \frac{dv}{dx} = \frac{v^3 + v^2 + v + 1}{1 - 2v - v^2} \) Hmm, okay. Let me write that as: \( \frac{dv}{dx} = \frac{v^3 + v^2 + v + 1}{x(1 - 2v - v^2)} \) So this is now a separable equation. I can separate variables by moving all terms involving v to one side and terms involving x to the other side. So: \( \frac{1 - 2v - v^2}{v^3 + v^2 + v + 1} dv = \frac{1}{x} dx \) Now, I need to integrate both sides. The right side is straightforward: \( \int \frac{1}{x} dx = \ln|x| + C \). The left side is more complicated. Let me focus on integrating \( \frac{1 - 2v - v^2}{v^3 + v^2 + v + 1} dv \). First, let's factor the denominator if possible. Let me try to factor \( v^3 + v^2 + v + 1 \). Factor by grouping: \( v^3 + v^2 + v + 1 = v^2(v + 1) + 1(v + 1) = (v^2 + 1)(v + 1) \) Yes! So denominator factors as \( (v + 1)(v^2 + 1) \). That helps. So the integrand becomes: \( \frac{1 - 2v - v^2}{(v + 1)(v^2 + 1)} dv \) Now, let me perform partial fraction decomposition on this rational function. Let's write: \( \frac{1 - 2v - v^2}{(v + 1)(v^2 + 1)} = \frac{A}{v + 1} + \frac{Bv + C}{v^2 + 1} \) Multiply both sides by \( (v + 1)(v^2 + 1) \): \( 1 - 2v - v^2 = A(v^2 + 1) + (Bv + C)(v + 1) \) Let me expand the right-hand side: A(v^2 + 1) + Bv(v + 1) + C(v + 1) = A v^2 + A + B v^2 + B v + C v + C = (A + B) v^2 + (B + C) v + (A + C) Set this equal to the left-hand side: \( -v^2 - 2v + 1 \) So equate coefficients: For \( v^2 \): A + B = -1 For \( v \): B + C = -2 For constant term: A + C = 1 Now, we have a system of equations: 1. A + B = -1 2. B + C = -2 3. A + C = 1 Let me solve this system. Let's subtract equation 3 from equation 1: (A + B) - (A + C) = -1 - 1 => B - C = -2 But from equation 2: B + C = -2 So we have: B - C = -2 B + C = -2 Adding these two equations: 2B = -4 => B = -2 Then from equation 2: -2 + C = -2 => C = 0 From equation 3: A + 0 = 1 => A = 1 So the partial fractions decomposition is: \( \frac{1}{v + 1} + \frac{-2v}{v^2 + 1} \) Therefore, the integral becomes: \( \int \left( \frac{1}{v + 1} - \frac{2v}{v^2 + 1} \right) dv \) Let's integrate term by term: 1. \( \int \frac{1}{v + 1} dv = \ln|v + 1| + C_1 \) 2. \( \int \frac{2v}{v^2 + 1} dv \). Let me substitute u = v^2 + 1, du = 2v dv. So this becomes \( \int \frac{du}{u} = \ln|u| + C_2 = \ln(v^2 + 1) + C_2 \). But since there's a negative sign, the integral becomes \( -\ln(v^2 + 1) + C_2 \) Putting it all together, the integral is: \( \ln|v + 1| - \ln(v^2 + 1) + C \) Combine the logs: \( \ln\left| \frac{v + 1}{v^2 + 1} \right| + C \) So, the left-hand side integral is \( \ln\left| \frac{v + 1}{v^2 + 1} \right| \), and the right-hand side integral is \( \ln|x| + C \). Combining the constants of integration, we can write: \( \ln\left| \frac{v + 1}{v^2 + 1} \right| = \ln|x| + C \) Exponentiating both sides to eliminate the logarithms: \( \left| \frac{v + 1}{v^2 + 1} \right| = C |x| \), where C is now a positive constant. Since we can write the constant as ±C, we can drop the absolute value signs and write: \( \frac{v + 1}{v^2 + 1} = K x \), where K is a constant (can be positive or negative). Combining the constants of integration, we can write: \( \ln\left| \frac{v + 1}{v^2 + 1} \right| = \ln|x| + C \) Exponentiating both sides to eliminate the logarithms: \( \left| \frac{v + 1}{v^2 + 1} \right| = C |x| \), where C is now a positive constant. Now, substitute back \( v = \frac{y}{x} \): \( \frac{\frac{y}{x} + 1}{\left( \frac{y}{x} \right)^2 + 1} = K x \) Simplify numerator and denominator: Numerator: \( \frac{y + x}{x} \) Denominator: \( \frac{y^2 + x^2}{x^2} \) So the entire expression becomes: \( \frac{(y + x)/x}{(y^2 + x^2)/x^2} = \frac{(y + x) x}{y^2 + x^2} = K x \) Simplify both sides: Left side: \( \frac{x(y + x)}{y^2 + x^2} \) Right side: \( K x \) Divide both sides by x (assuming x ≠ 0, which is valid here since the initial condition is at x=1): \( \frac{y + x}{y^2 + x^2} = K \) So the equation simplifies to: \( y + x = K(y^2 + x^2) \) Now, apply the initial condition \( y(1) = -1 \). Substitute x = 1, y = -1 into the equation: Left side: (-1) + 1 = 0 Right side: K((-1)^2 + 1^2) = K(1 + 1) = 2K Therefore, 0 = 2K => K = 0 Wait, but if K = 0, then the equation becomes y + x = 0, so y = -x. Let me check if this satisfies the original differential equation. If y = -x, then dy/dx = -1. Let's compute the right-hand side of the original equation: Numerator: x^2 + 2x(-x) - (-x)^2 = x^2 - 2x^2 - x^2 = -2x^2 Denominator: x^2 - 2x(-x) - (-x)^2 = x^2 + 2x^2 - x^2 = 2x^2 Therefore, RHS = -2x^2 / 2x^2 = -1, which matches dy/dx = -1. So y = -x is indeed a solution. But wait, according to our previous steps, K = 0 gives y + x = 0, which is y = -x. So that's the solution? But let me check: when we exponentiated, we assumed that the constant K is non-zero, but in this case, K turned out to be zero. When solving the integral, we had: \( \ln\left| \frac{v + 1}{v^2 + 1} \right| = \ln|x| + C \) Exponentiating both sides gives: \( \frac{|v + 1|}{v^2 + 1} = C |x| \), where C is a positive constant. We use the substitution \( v = \frac{y}{x} \), which transforms the equation into: \[ v + x \frac{dv}{dx} = \frac{1 + 2v - v^2}{1 - 2v - v^2} \] Rearranging and simplifying, we get: \[ x \frac{dv}{dx} = \frac{v^3 + v^2 + v + 1}{1 - 2v - v^2} \] Separating variables, we have: \[ \frac{1 - 2v - v^2}{v^3 + v^2 + v + 1} dv = \frac{1}{x} dx \] Factoring the denominator \( v^3 + v^2 + v + 1 = (v + 1)(v^2 + 1) \) and using partial fraction decomposition, we integrate both sides: \[ \int \left( \frac{1}{v + 1} - \frac{2v}{v^2 + 1} \right) dv = \int \frac{1}{x} dx \] This results in: \[ \ln\left| \frac{v + 1}{v^2 + 1} \right| = \ln|x| + C \] Exponentiating both sides, we get: \[ \frac{v + 1}{v^2 + 1} = Kx \] Substituting back \( v = \frac{y}{x} \), we have: \[ \frac{y + x}{y^2 + x^2} = K \] Applying the initial condition \( y(1) = -1 \), we find \( K = 0 \), leading to the solution \( y = -x \). Verifying this solution, we check that it satisfies both the differential equation and the initial condition. Thus, the solution is: \[ \boxed{y = -x} \]

Problem: Solve the differential equation \( \frac{dy}{dx}=\frac{x^2+2xy-y^2}{x^2-2xy-y^2} \) with the initial condition \( y(1)=-1 \). Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Reference Answer: Okay, so I need to solve this differential equation: \( \frac{dy}{dx} = \frac{x^2 + 2xy - y^2}{x^2 - 2xy - y^2} \) with the initial condition \( y(1) = -1 \). Hmm, let's start by analyzing the equation to see what kind it is and what method might work here. First, both the numerator and the denominator are quadratic in x and y. That makes me think it might be a homogeneous equation. Let me check if that's true. A differential equation is homogeneous if it can be written as \( \frac{dy}{dx} = F\left( \frac{y}{x} \right) \). So let's see if substituting \( v = \frac{y}{x} \) would simplify things. Let me rewrite the equation. The numerator is \( x^2 + 2xy - y^2 \) and the denominator is \( x^2 - 2xy - y^2 \). Let me factor out \( x^2 \) from both numerator and denominator: Numerator: \( x^2 \left( 1 + 2\left( \frac{y}{x} \right) - \left( \frac{y}{x} \right)^2 \right) \) Denominator: \( x^2 \left( 1 - 2\left( \frac{y}{x} \right) - \left( \frac{y}{x} \right)^2 \right) \) So when I divide them, the \( x^2 \) terms cancel out, and I get: \( \frac{dy}{dx} = \frac{1 + 2v - v^2}{1 - 2v - v^2} \), where \( v = \frac{y}{x} \). Alright, so this substitution simplifies the equation. Now, since it's homogeneous, we can use the substitution \( y = vx \), which implies \( \frac{dy}{dx} = v + x \frac{dv}{dx} \). Let me write that down: \( v + x \frac{dv}{dx} = \frac{1 + 2v - v^2}{1 - 2v - v^2} \) So now, I need to solve for \( v \). Let's rearrange the equation to isolate \( \frac{dv}{dx} \): \( x \frac{dv}{dx} = \frac{1 + 2v - v^2}{1 - 2v - v^2} - v \) Let me compute the right-hand side. To subtract these fractions, I need a common denominator: \( \frac{1 + 2v - v^2 - v(1 - 2v - v^2)}{1 - 2v - v^2} \) Expanding the numerator: First term: \( 1 + 2v - v^2 \) Second term: \( -v + 2v^2 + v^3 \) Combine them: 1 + 2v - v^2 - v + 2v^2 + v^3 Combine like terms: 1 + (2v - v) + (-v^2 + 2v^2) + v^3 Which simplifies to: 1 + v + v^2 + v^3 So the equation becomes: \( x \frac{dv}{dx} = \frac{v^3 + v^2 + v + 1}{1 - 2v - v^2} \) Hmm, okay. Let me write that as: \( \frac{dv}{dx} = \frac{v^3 + v^2 + v + 1}{x(1 - 2v - v^2)} \) So this is now a separable equation. I can separate variables by moving all terms involving v to one side and terms involving x to the other side. So: \( \frac{1 - 2v - v^2}{v^3 + v^2 + v + 1} dv = \frac{1}{x} dx \) Now, I need to integrate both sides. The right side is straightforward: \( \int \frac{1}{x} dx = \ln|x| + C \). The left side is more complicated. Let me focus on integrating \( \frac{1 - 2v - v^2}{v^3 + v^2 + v + 1} dv \). First, let's factor the denominator if possible. Let me try to factor \( v^3 + v^2 + v + 1 \). Factor by grouping: \( v^3 + v^2 + v + 1 = v^2(v + 1) + 1(v + 1) = (v^2 + 1)(v + 1) \) Yes! So denominator factors as \( (v + 1)(v^2 + 1) \). That helps. So the integrand becomes: \( \frac{1 - 2v - v^2}{(v + 1)(v^2 + 1)} dv \) Now, let me perform partial fraction decomposition on this rational function. Let's write: \( \frac{1 - 2v - v^2}{(v + 1)(v^2 + 1)} = \frac{A}{v + 1} + \frac{Bv + C}{v^2 + 1} \) Multiply both sides by \( (v + 1)(v^2 + 1) \): \( 1 - 2v - v^2 = A(v^2 + 1) + (Bv + C)(v + 1) \) Let me expand the right-hand side: A(v^2 + 1) + Bv(v + 1) + C(v + 1) = A v^2 + A + B v^2 + B v + C v + C = (A + B) v^2 + (B + C) v + (A + C) Set this equal to the left-hand side: \( -v^2 - 2v + 1 \) So equate coefficients: For \( v^2 \): A + B = -1 For \( v \): B + C = -2 For constant term: A + C = 1 Now, we have a system of equations: 1. Let me substitute u = v^2 + 1, du = 2v dv. So this becomes \( \int \frac{du}{u} = \ln|u| + C_2 = \ln(v^2 + 1) + C_2 \). But since there's a negative sign, the integral becomes \( -\ln(v^2 + 1) + C_2 \) Putting it all together, the integral is: \( \ln|v + 1| - \ln(v^2 + 1) + C \) Combine the logs: \( \ln\left| \frac{v + 1}{v^2 + 1} \right| + C \) So, the left-hand side integral is \( \ln\left| \frac{v + 1}{v^2 + 1} \right| \), and the right-hand side integral is \( \ln|x| + C \). Combining the constants of integration, we can write: \( \ln\left| \frac{v + 1}{v^2 + 1} \right| = \ln|x| + C \) Exponentiating both sides to eliminate the logarithms: \( \left| \frac{v + 1}{v^2 + 1} \right| = C |x| \), where C is now a positive constant. Since we can write the constant as ±C, we can drop the absolute value signs and write: \( \frac{v + 1}{v^2 + 1} = K x \), where K is a constant (can be positive or negative). Now, substitute back \( v = \frac{y}{x} \): \( \frac{\frac{y}{x} + 1}{\left( \frac{y}{x} \right)^2 + 1} = K x \) Simplify numerator and denominator: Numerator: \( \frac{y + x}{x} \) Denominator: \( \frac{y^2 + x^2}{x^2} \) So the entire expression becomes: \( \frac{(y + x)/x}{(y^2 + x^2)/x^2} = \frac{(y + x) x}{y^2 + x^2} = K x \) Simplify both sides: Left side: \( \frac{x(y + x)}{y^2 + x^2} \) Right side: \( K x \) Divide both sides by x (assuming x ≠ 0, which is valid here since the initial condition is at x=1): \( \frac{y + x}{y^2 + x^2} = K \) So the equation simplifies to: \( y + x = K(y^2 + x^2) \) Now, apply the initial condition \( y(1) = -1 \). Substitute x = 1, y = -1 into the equation: Left side: (-1) + 1 = 0 Right side: K((-1)^2 + 1^2) = K(1 + 1) = 2K Therefore, 0 = 2K => K = 0 Wait, but if K = 0, then the equation becomes y + x = 0, so y = -x. Let's compute the right-hand side of the original equation: Numerator: x^2 + 2x(-x) - (-x)^2 = x^2 - 2x^2 - x^2 = -2x^2 Denominator: x^2 - 2x(-x) - (-x)^2 = x^2 + 2x^2 - x^2 = 2x^2 Therefore, RHS = -2x^2 / 2x^2 = -1, which matches dy/dx = -1. So y = -x is indeed a solution. But wait, according to our previous steps, K = 0 gives y + x = 0, which is y = -x. So that's the solution? We use the substitution \( v = \frac{y}{x} \), which transforms the equation into: \[ v + x \frac{dv}{dx} = \frac{1 + 2v - v^2}{1 - 2v - v^2} \] Rearranging and simplifying, we get: \[ x \frac{dv}{dx} = \frac{v^3 + v^2 + v + 1}{1 - 2v - v^2} \] Separating variables, we have: \[ \frac{1 - 2v - v^2}{v^3 + v^2 + v + 1} dv = \frac{1}{x} dx \] Factoring the denominator \( v^3 + v^2 + v + 1 = (v + 1)(v^2 + 1) \) and using partial fraction decomposition, we integrate both sides: \[ \int \left( \frac{1}{v + 1} - \frac{2v}{v^2 + 1} \right) dv = \int \frac{1}{x} dx \] This results in: \[ \ln\left| \frac{v + 1}{v^2 + 1} \right| = \ln|x| + C \] Exponentiating both sides, we get: \[ \frac{v + 1}{v^2 + 1} = Kx \] Substituting back \( v = \frac{y}{x} \), we have: \[ \frac{y + x}{y^2 + x^2} = K \] Applying the initial condition \( y(1) = -1 \), we find \( K = 0 \), leading to the solution \( y = -x \). Verifying this solution, we check that it satisfies both the differential equation and the initial condition. Thus, the solution is: \[ \boxed{y = -x} \]

Let's write: \( \frac{1 - 2v - v^2}{(v + 1)(v^2 + 1)} = \frac{A}{v + 1} + \frac{Bv + C}{v^2 + 1} \) Multiply both sides by \( (v + 1)(v^2 + 1) \): \( 1 - 2v - v^2 = A(v^2 + 1) + (Bv + C)(v + 1) \) Let me expand the right-hand side: A(v^2 + 1) + Bv(v + 1) + C(v + 1) = A v^2 + A + B v^2 + B v + C v + C = (A + B) v^2 + (B + C) v + (A + C) Set this equal to the left-hand side: \( -v^2 - 2v + 1 \) So equate coefficients: For \( v^2 \): A + B = -1 For \( v \): B + C = -2 For constant term: A + C = 1 Now, we have a system of equations: 1. A + B = -1 2. B + C = -2 3. A + C = 1 Let me solve this system. Let's subtract equation 3 from equation 1: (A + B) - (A + C) = -1 - 1 => B - C = -2 But from equation 2: B + C = -2 So we have: B - C = -2 B + C = -2 Adding these two equations: 2B = -4 => B = -2 Then from equation 2: -2 + C = -2 => C = 0 From equation 3: A + 0 = 1 => A = 1 So the partial fractions decomposition is: \( \frac{1}{v + 1} + \frac{-2v}{v^2 + 1} \) Therefore, the integral becomes: \( \int \left( \frac{1}{v + 1} - \frac{2v}{v^2 + 1} \right) dv \) Let's integrate term by term: 1. \( \int \frac{1}{v + 1} dv = \ln|v + 1| + C_1 \) 2. \( \int \frac{2v}{v^2 + 1} dv \). Let me substitute u = v^2 + 1, du = 2v dv. So this becomes \( \int \frac{du}{u} = \ln|u| + C_2 = \ln(v^2 + 1) + C_2 \). But since there's a negative sign, the integral becomes \( -\ln(v^2 + 1) + C_2 \) Putting it all together, the integral is: \( \ln|v + 1| - \ln(v^2 + 1) + C \) Combine the logs: \( \ln\left| \frac{v + 1}{v^2 + 1} \right| + C \) So, the left-hand side integral is \( \ln\left| \frac{v + 1}{v^2 + 1} \right| \), and the right-hand side integral is \( \ln|x| + C \). Combining the constants of integration, we can write: \( \ln\left| \frac{v + 1}{v^2 + 1} \right| = \ln|x| + C \) Exponentiating both sides to eliminate the logarithms: \( \left| \frac{v + 1}{v^2 + 1} \right| = C |x| \), where C is now a positive constant. Since we can write the constant as ±C, we can drop the absolute value signs and write: \( \frac{v + 1}{v^2 + 1} = K x \), where K is a constant (can be positive or negative). Now, substitute back \( v = \frac{y}{x} \): \( \frac{\frac{y}{x} + 1}{\left( \frac{y}{x} \right)^2 + 1} = K x \) Simplify numerator and denominator: Numerator: \( \frac{y + x}{x} \) Denominator: \( \frac{y^2 + x^2}{x^2} \) So the entire expression becomes: \( \frac{(y + x)/x}{(y^2 + x^2)/x^2} = \frac{(y + x) x}{y^2 + x^2} = K x \) Simplify both sides: Left side: \( \frac{x(y + x)}{y^2 + x^2} \) Right side: \( K x \) Divide both sides by x (assuming x ≠ 0, which is valid here since the initial condition is at x=1): \( \frac{y + x}{y^2 + x^2} = K \) So the equation simplifies to: \( y + x = K(y^2 + x^2) \) Now, apply the initial condition \( y(1) = -1 \). Substitute x = 1, y = -1 into the equation: Left side: (-1) + 1 = 0 Right side: K((-1)^2 + 1^2) = K(1 + 1) = 2K Therefore, 0 = 2K => K = 0 Wait, but if K = 0, then the equation becomes y + x = 0, so y = -x. Let me check if this satisfies the original differential equation. If y = -x, then dy/dx = -1. Let's compute the right-hand side of the original equation: Numerator: x^2 + 2x(-x) - (-x)^2 = x^2 - 2x^2 - x^2 = -2x^2 Denominator: x^2 - 2x(-x) - (-x)^2 = x^2 + 2x^2 - x^2 = 2x^2 Therefore, RHS = -2x^2 / 2x^2 = -1, which matches dy/dx = -1. So y = -x is indeed a solution. But wait, according to our previous steps, K = 0 gives y + x = 0, which is y = -x. So that's the solution? Therefore, y = -x is the unique solution. But let me think again. When solving the integral, we had: \( \ln\left| \frac{v + 1}{v^2 + 1} \right| = \ln|x| + C \) Exponentiating both sides gives: \( \frac{|v + 1|}{v^2 + 1} = C |x| \), where C is a positive constant. Then we can write: \( \frac{v + 1}{v^2 + 1} = \pm C x = K x \), where K is any real constant (including zero). But in our case, the initial condition forced K to be zero. So even if the general solution is \( \frac{v + 1}{v^2 + 1} = K x \), but K is arbitrary, but with the initial condition x=1, y=-1, so v = y/x = -1/1 = -1. Substituting into the equation: \( \frac{-1 + 1}{(-1)^2 + 1} = \frac{0}{2} = 0 = K * 1 \), so K=0. Therefore, only the solution with K=0 is valid here, leading to y = -x. Hence, the solution to the initial value problem is y = -x. But wait, let's confirm this with the original differential equation. Let's take y = -x. Then dy/dx = -1. Let's compute the right-hand side: Numerator: x^2 + 2x(-x) - (-x)^2 = x^2 - 2x^2 - x^2 = -2x^2 Denominator: x^2 - 2x(-x) - (-x)^2 = x^2 + 2x^2 - x^2 = 2x^2 Thus, RHS = -2x^2 / 2x^2 = -1, which matches dy/dx = -1. To write it in the box, it's \boxed{y = -x}. **Final Answer** \boxed{y = -x} </think> To solve the differential equation \( \frac{dy}{dx} = \frac{x^2 + 2xy - y^2}{x^2 - 2xy - y^2} \) with the initial condition \( y(1) = -1 \), we start by recognizing that it is a homogeneous equation. We use the substitution \( v = \frac{y}{x} \), which transforms the equation into: \[ v + x \frac{dv}{dx} = \frac{1 + 2v - v^2}{1 - 2v - v^2} \] Rearranging and simplifying, we get: \[ x \frac{dv}{dx} = \frac{v^3 + v^2 + v + 1}{1 - 2v - v^2} \] Separating variables, we have: \[ \frac{1 - 2v - v^2}{v^3 + v^2 + v + 1} dv = \frac{1}{x} dx \] Factoring the denominator \( v^3 + v^2 + v + 1 = (v + 1)(v^2 + 1) \) and using partial fraction decomposition, we integrate both sides: \[ \int \left( \frac{1}{v + 1} - \frac{2v}{v^2 + 1} \right) dv = \int \frac{1}{x} dx \] This results in: \[ \ln\left| \frac{v + 1}{v^2 + 1} \right| = \ln|x| + C \] Exponentiating both sides, we get: \[ \frac{v + 1}{v^2 + 1} = Kx \] Substituting back \( v = \frac{y}{x} \), we have: \[ \frac{y + x}{y^2 + x^2} = K \] Applying the initial condition \( y(1) = -1 \), we find \( K = 0 \), leading to the solution \( y = -x \). Verifying this solution, we check that it satisfies both the differential equation and the initial condition. Thus, the solution is: \[ \boxed{y = -x} \]

Problem: Consider two unknowns $y \in \mathcal{Y}$ and $s \in \mathcal{S}$. Let $f$ be a function of $(y,s)$ and $g$ be a function of $s$. Suppose the maximizers of the problem \( \max_{y \in \mathcal{Y}, s \in \mathcal{S}} f(y,s) - g(s) \) are unique and denoted by $(y^*, s^*)$. Now consider the problem \( \max_{y \in \mathcal{Y}} f(y, s^*) \) with a unique maximizer. Is it true that the maximizer of this problem, \( \arg\max_{y \in \mathcal{Y}} f(y, s^*) \), is equal to $y^*$? Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: Yes \boxed{y^*}

Reference Answer: Alright, let's tackle this problem step by step. So, we have two unknowns, y and s, each in their own sets 𝒴 and 𝒮. The main goal is to maximize the function f(y, s) minus g(s). They've told us that the maximizers for this joint problem are unique and are denoted by (y*, s*). Then, we have another problem where we fix s at s* and just maximize f(y, s*) over y, and they want to know if the maximizer here is still y*. Hmm, okay. Let me think. So, the first thing that comes to mind is that when you maximize jointly over y and s, you're considering all possible combinations of y and s. So, (y*, s*) is the pair that gives the highest value of f(y,s) - g(s). Now, if we fix s at s* and then maximize over y, we're essentially looking for the best y given that s is already set to s*. But the question is whether this y will be the same y* as before. Intuitively, since s* was part of the optimal pair, maybe the y that was optimal when s was s* would still be optimal when we fix s at s*. But wait, is there any reason it might not be? Let me formalize this. Let's denote the joint optimization problem as: (y*, s*) = argmax_{y ∈ 𝒴, s ∈ 𝒮} [f(y, s) - g(s)] Then, the second problem is: y' = argmax_{y ∈ 𝒴} f(y, s*) We need to check if y' = y*. Since (y*, s*) is the unique maximizer of the joint problem, for all other y and s, f(y, s) - g(s) ≤ f(y*, s*) - g(s*). In particular, if we fix s = s*, then for any y, f(y, s*) - g(s*) ≤ f(y*, s*) - g(s*). But since g(s*) is a constant when we fix s = s*, maximizing f(y, s*) over y is equivalent to maximizing [f(y, s*) - g(s*)] over y, because subtracting a constant doesn't change the location of the maximum. Therefore, the y that maximizes f(y, s*) should be the same y* that was part of the joint maximizer. Wait, is that right? Let me double-check. If we have f(y, s) - g(s) and we fix s = s*, then the problem becomes max_y [f(y, s*) - g(s*)]. Since g(s*) is fixed, the maximizer y must be the same as the one that maximizes f(y, s*). But in the joint optimization problem, when s is chosen as s*, the corresponding y that maximizes the expression is y*. Therefore, y* must be the maximizer of f(y, s*) over y. But hold on, is there a scenario where even though (y*, s*) is the joint maximizer, there could be another y that gives a higher f(y, s*) when s is fixed? Let's suppose, for contradiction, that there exists some y' ≠ y* such that f(y', s*) > f(y*, s*). Then, if we consider the joint problem, f(y', s*) - g(s*) would be greater than f(y*, s*) - g(s*), which contradicts the fact that (y*, s*) is the maximizer of the joint problem. Therefore, such a y' cannot exist, and thus y* must indeed be the maximizer of f(y, s*) over y. But the user mentioned that the maximizers are unique. That’s important. Suppose the joint maximizer is unique. Then, in the second problem, they also state that the maximizer is unique. So, uniqueness here is key. If there were multiple maximizers, maybe there could be different y's when you fix s at s*, but since they are unique, the reasoning above should hold. Let me think of an example. Let’s take simple functions where I can compute everything explicitly. Let 𝒴 and 𝒮 be real numbers. Suppose f(y, s) = - (y - s)^2, and g(s) = s^2. Then, the joint optimization problem is max_{y,s} [ - (y - s)^2 - s^2 ]. Let's compute this. First, expand the expression: - (y^2 - 2ys + s^2) - s^2 = -y^2 + 2ys - s^2 - s^2 = -y^2 + 2ys - 2s^2. To find the maximum, take partial derivatives with respect to y and s. ∂/∂y = -2y + 2s = 0 ⇒ y = s ∂/∂s = 2y - 4s = 0 From the first equation, y = s. Substitute into the second equation: 2s - 4s = -2s = 0 ⇒ s = 0. Therefore, y = 0. So, the unique maximizer is (y*, s*) = (0, 0). Now, let's fix s* = 0 and maximize f(y, 0) over y. f(y, 0) = - (y - 0)^2 = -y^2. The maximum is at y = 0, which is y*. So in this case, it works. Another example. Let f(y, s) = y*s and g(s) = s^2. Suppose 𝒴 and 𝒮 are real numbers. Then the joint optimization is max_{y,s} [ y*s - s^2 ]. Let's compute this. Take partial derivatives: ∂/∂y = s = 0 ⇒ s = 0 ∂/∂s = y - 2s = 0 From the first equation, s = 0. Then the second equation gives y = 0. So (y*, s*) = (0, 0). Now, fix s* = 0, and maximize f(y, 0) = y*0 = 0. So any y is a maximizer, but since the problem says the maximizer is unique, maybe this example isn't suitable because when s* = 0, all y give the same value. Then, fixing s* = 0, maximize f(y, 0) = -y² - 0 = -y². So maximizer is y = 0, which is y*. So that works. Alternatively, let’s consider a case where s is chosen to affect both f and g. Suppose f(y, s) = y - (s - 1)^2 and g(s) = 0. Then the joint maximization is max_{y,s} [ y - (s - 1)^2 ]. The maximum occurs when y is as large as possible and (s - 1)^2 is as small as possible. Suppose 𝒴 is [0, ∞) and 𝒮 is ℝ. Then, to maximize y - (s -1)^2, set y to infinity, but if 𝒴 is bounded, say 𝒴 = [0, 1], then set y = 1 and s = 1. Then (y*, s*) = (1,1). Then, fix s* =1 and maximize f(y, 1) = y - 0 = y. So maximizer is y=1, which is y*. But what if the functions are more intertwined? Suppose f(y, s) = -(y - s)^2 and g(s) = -(s - a)^2 for some a. Wait, then the joint maximization would be max_{y,s} [ -(y - s)^2 + (s - a)^2 ]. Let’s compute this. Expanding: -y² + 2ys - s² + s² - 2as + a² = -y² + 2ys - 2as + a². Take partial derivatives: ∂/∂y = -2y + 2s = 0 ⇒ y = s ∂/∂s = 2y - 2a = 0 ⇒ y = a Thus, y = s = a. So (y*, s*) = (a, a). Then, if we fix s* = a and maximize f(y, a) = -(y - a)^2, which is maximized at y = a, which is y*. So again, it works. Wait, maybe the answer is yes? When we fix s* and then optimize over y, we are no longer considering the cost g(s), but in the original problem, s* was chosen to maximize f(y,s) - g(s). However, when we fix s*, we just maximize f(y, s*). But since (y*, s*) is the maximizer of f(y,s) - g(s), for that particular s*, the y that maximizes f(y,s*) must be y*, because if there was a better y for that s*, then the pair (y', s*) would give a higher value of f(y,s) - g(s), contradicting the optimality of (y*, s*). Therefore, the logic is that since (y*, s*) is the unique maximizer of the joint problem, then for s = s*, the optimal y is y*, otherwise, if there was a better y for s*, then (y', s*) would be a better pair, which can't happen. Therefore, yes, the maximizer of the second problem is y*. Therefore, the answer should be yes, it is true that the maximizer of the problem max_{y ∈ 𝒴} f(y, s*) is y*. **Final Answer** \boxed{Yes} </think> Given two unknowns \( y \in \mathcal{Y} \) and \( s \in \mathcal{S} \), we consider the joint optimization problem \( \max_{y \in \mathcal{Y}, s \in \mathcal{S}} f(y,s) - g(s) \) with unique maximizers \( (y^*, s^*) \). We need to determine if the maximizer of the problem \( \max_{y \in \mathcal{Y}} f(y, s^*) \) is equal to \( y^* \). 1. **Joint Optimization Problem**: The pair \( (y^*, s^*) \) is the unique maximizer of \( f(y, s) - g(s) \). This means for all other \( y \in \mathcal{Y} \) and \( s \in \mathcal{S} \), we have: \[ f(y, s) - g(s) \leq f(y^*, s^*) - g(s^*) \] 2. **Fixed \( s^* \) Optimization**: When fixing \( s = s^* \), we need to maximize \( f(y, s^*) \) over \( y \). Since \( g(s^*) \) is a constant, maximizing \( f(y, s^*) \) is equivalent to maximizing \( f(y, s^*) - g(s^*) \). 3. **Contradiction Argument**: Suppose there exists a \( y' \neq y^* \) such that \( f(y', s^*) > f(y^*, s^*) \). Then, the pair \( (y', s^*) \) would yield a higher value for the joint optimization problem: \[ f(y', s^*) - g(s^*) > f(y^*, s^*) - g(s^*) \] This contradicts the uniqueness of \( (y^*, s^*) \) as the maximizer of the joint problem. Therefore, no such \( y' \) can exist. 4. **Conclusion**: Since \( (y^*, s^*) \) is the unique maximizer of the joint problem, \( y^* \) must be the unique maximizer of \( f(y, s^*) \) when \( s \) is fixed at \( s^* \). Thus, the maximizer of the problem \( \max_{y \in \mathcal{Y}} f(y, s^*) \) is indeed \( y^* \). \[ \boxed{Yes} \]

Suppose f(y, s) = y - (s - 1)^2 and g(s) = 0. Then the joint maximization is max_{y,s} [ y - (s - 1)^2 ]. The maximum occurs when y is as large as possible and (s - 1)^2 is as small as possible. Suppose 𝒴 is [0, ∞) and 𝒮 is ℝ. Then, to maximize y - (s -1)^2, set y to infinity, but if 𝒴 is bounded, say 𝒴 = [0, 1], then set y = 1 and s = 1. Then (y*, s*) = (1,1). Then, fix s* =1 and maximize f(y, 1) = y - 0 = y. So maximizer is y=1, which is y*. But what if the functions are more intertwined? Suppose f(y, s) = -(y - s)^2 and g(s) = -(s - a)^2 for some a. Wait, then the joint maximization would be max_{y,s} [ -(y - s)^2 + (s - a)^2 ]. Let’s compute this. Expanding: -y² + 2ys - s² + s² - 2as + a² = -y² + 2ys - 2as + a². Take partial derivatives: ∂/∂y = -2y + 2s = 0 ⇒ y = s ∂/∂s = 2y - 2a = 0 ⇒ y = a Thus, y = s = a. So (y*, s*) = (a, a). Then, if we fix s* = a and maximize f(y, a) = -(y - a)^2, which is maximized at y = a, which is y*. So again, it works. Wait, maybe the answer is yes? But the question seems to suggest there might be a trick. Let me think again. Suppose that in the joint optimization problem, the optimal s* is chosen considering the cost g(s). When we fix s* and then optimize over y, we are no longer considering the cost g(s), but in the original problem, s* was chosen to maximize f(y,s) - g(s). However, when we fix s*, we just maximize f(y, s*). But since (y*, s*) is the maximizer of f(y,s) - g(s), for that particular s*, the y that maximizes f(y,s*) must be y*, because if there was a better y for that s*, then the pair (y', s*) would give a higher value of f(y,s) - g(s), contradicting the optimality of (y*, s*). Therefore, the logic is that since (y*, s*) is the unique maximizer of the joint problem, then for s = s*, the optimal y is y*, otherwise, if there was a better y for s*, then (y', s*) would be a better pair, which can't happen. Therefore, yes, the maximizer of the second problem is y*. Therefore, the answer should be yes, it is true that the maximizer of the problem max_{y ∈ 𝒴} f(y, s*) is y*. **Final Answer** \boxed{Yes} </think> Given two unknowns \( y \in \mathcal{Y} \) and \( s \in \mathcal{S} \), we consider the joint optimization problem \( \max_{y \in \mathcal{Y}, s \in \mathcal{S}} f(y,s) - g(s) \) with unique maximizers \( (y^*, s^*) \). We need to determine if the maximizer of the problem \( \max_{y \in \mathcal{Y}} f(y, s^*) \) is equal to \( y^* \). 1. **Joint Optimization Problem**: The pair \( (y^*, s^*) \) is the unique maximizer of \( f(y, s) - g(s) \). This means for all other \( y \in \mathcal{Y} \) and \( s \in \mathcal{S} \), we have: \[ f(y, s) - g(s) \leq f(y^*, s^*) - g(s^*) \] 2. **Fixed \( s^* \) Optimization**: When fixing \( s = s^* \), we need to maximize \( f(y, s^*) \) over \( y \). Since \( g(s^*) \) is a constant, maximizing \( f(y, s^*) \) is equivalent to maximizing \( f(y, s^*) - g(s^*) \). 3. **Contradiction Argument**: Suppose there exists a \( y' \neq y^* \) such that \( f(y', s^*) > f(y^*, s^*) \). Then, the pair \( (y', s^*) \) would yield a higher value for the joint optimization problem: \[ f(y', s^*) - g(s^*) > f(y^*, s^*) - g(s^*) \] This contradicts the uniqueness of \( (y^*, s^*) \) as the maximizer of the joint problem. Therefore, no such \( y' \) can exist. 4. **Conclusion**: Since \( (y^*, s^*) \) is the unique maximizer of the joint problem, \( y^* \) must be the unique maximizer of \( f(y, s^*) \) when \( s \) is fixed at \( s^* \). Thus, the maximizer of the problem \( \max_{y \in \mathcal{Y}} f(y, s^*) \) is indeed \( y^* \). \[ \boxed{Yes} \]

Problem: Consider two unknowns $y \in \mathcal{Y}$ and $s \in \mathcal{S}$. Let $f$ be a function of $(y,s)$ and $g$ be a function of $s$. Suppose the maximizers of the problem \( \max_{y \in \mathcal{Y}, s \in \mathcal{S}} f(y,s) - g(s) \) are unique and denoted by $(y^*, s^*)$. Now consider the problem \( \max_{y \in \mathcal{Y}} f(y, s^*) \) with a unique maximizer. Is it true that the maximizer of this problem, \( \arg\max_{y \in \mathcal{Y}} f(y, s^*) \), is equal to $y^*$? Write your answer in \boxed{} format.
