Reference Answer: Alright, let's tackle this problem step by step. So, we have two unknowns, y and s, each in their own sets 𝒴 and 𝒮. The main goal is to maximize the function f(y, s) minus g(s). They've told us that the maximizers for this joint problem are unique and are denoted by (y*, s*). Then, we have another problem where we fix s at s* and just maximize f(y, s*) over y, and they want to know if the maximizer here is still y*. Hmm, okay. Let me think. So, the first thing that comes to mind is that when you maximize jointly over y and s, you're considering all possible combinations of y and s. So, (y*, s*) is the pair that gives the highest value of f(y,s) - g(s). Now, if we fix s at s* and then maximize over y, we're essentially looking for the best y given that s is already set to s*. But the question is whether this y will be the same y* as before. Intuitively, since s* was part of the optimal pair, maybe the y that was optimal when s was s* would still be optimal when we fix s at s*. But wait, is there any reason it might not be? Let me formalize this. Let's denote the joint optimization problem as: (y*, s*) = argmax_{y ∈ 𝒴, s ∈ 𝒮} [f(y, s) - g(s)] Then, the second problem is: y' = argmax_{y ∈ 𝒴} f(y, s*) We need to check if y' = y*. Since (y*, s*) is the unique maximizer of the joint problem, for all other y and s, f(y, s) - g(s) ≤ f(y*, s*) - g(s*). In particular, if we fix s = s*, then for any y, f(y, s*) - g(s*) ≤ f(y*, s*) - g(s*). Therefore, the answer should be yes, it is true that the maximizer of the problem max_{y ∈ 𝒴} f(y, s*) is y*. **Final Answer** \boxed{Yes} </think> Given two unknowns \( y \in \mathcal{Y} \) and \( s \in \mathcal{S} \), we consider the joint optimization problem \( \max_{y \in \mathcal{Y}, s \in \mathcal{S}} f(y,s) - g(s) \) with unique maximizers \( (y^*, s^*) \). We need to determine if the maximizer of the problem \( \max_{y \in \mathcal{Y}} f(y, s^*) \) is equal to \( y^* \). 1. **Joint Optimization Problem**: The pair \( (y^*, s^*) \) is the unique maximizer of \( f(y, s) - g(s) \). This means for all other \( y \in \mathcal{Y} \) and \( s \in \mathcal{S} \), we have: \[ f(y, s) - g(s) \leq f(y^*, s^*) - g(s^*) \] 2. **Fixed \( s^* \) Optimization**: When fixing \( s = s^* \), we need to maximize \( f(y, s^*) \) over \( y \). Since \( g(s^*) \) is a constant, maximizing \( f(y, s^*) \) is equivalent to maximizing \( f(y, s^*) - g(s^*) \). 3. **Contradiction Argument**: Suppose there exists a \( y' \neq y^* \) such that \( f(y', s^*) > f(y^*, s^*) \). Then, the pair \( (y', s^*) \) would yield a higher value for the joint optimization problem: \[ f(y', s^*) - g(s^*) > f(y^*, s^*) - g(s^*) \] This contradicts the uniqueness of \( (y^*, s^*) \) as the maximizer of the joint problem. Therefore, no such \( y' \) can exist. 4. **Conclusion**: Since \( (y^*, s^*) \) is the unique maximizer of the joint problem, \( y^* \) must be the unique maximizer of \( f(y, s^*) \) when \( s \) is fixed at \( s^* \). Thus, the maximizer of the problem \( \max_{y \in \mathcal{Y}} f(y, s^*) \) is indeed \( y^* \). \[ \boxed{Yes} \]

Problem: Consider two unknowns $y \in \mathcal{Y}$ and $s \in \mathcal{S}$. Let $f$ be a function of $(y,s)$ and $g$ be a function of $s$. Suppose the maximizers of the problem \( \max_{y \in \mathcal{Y}, s \in \mathcal{S}} f(y,s) - g(s) \) are unique and denoted by $(y^*, s^*)$. Now consider the problem \( \max_{y \in \mathcal{Y}} f(y, s^*) \) with a unique maximizer. Is it true that the maximizer of this problem, \( \arg\max_{y \in \mathcal{Y}} f(y, s^*) \), is equal to $y^*$? Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: Yes \boxed{y^*}

Reference Answer: Alright, let's tackle this problem step by step. So, we have two unknowns, y and s, each in their own sets 𝒴 and 𝒮. The main goal is to maximize the function f(y, s) minus g(s). They've told us that the maximizers for this joint problem are unique and are denoted by (y*, s*). Then, we have another problem where we fix s at s* and just maximize f(y, s*) over y, and they want to know if the maximizer here is still y*. Hmm, okay. Let me think. So, the first thing that comes to mind is that when you maximize jointly over y and s, you're considering all possible combinations of y and s. So, (y*, s*) is the pair that gives the highest value of f(y,s) - g(s). Now, if we fix s at s* and then maximize over y, we're essentially looking for the best y given that s is already set to s*. But the question is whether this y will be the same y* as before. Intuitively, since s* was part of the optimal pair, maybe the y that was optimal when s was s* would still be optimal when we fix s at s*. But wait, is there any reason it might not be? Let me formalize this. Let's denote the joint optimization problem as: (y*, s*) = argmax_{y ∈ 𝒴, s ∈ 𝒮} [f(y, s) - g(s)] Then, the second problem is: y' = argmax_{y ∈ 𝒴} f(y, s*) We need to check if y' = y*. Since (y*, s*) is the unique maximizer of the joint problem, for all other y and s, f(y, s) - g(s) ≤ f(y*, s*) - g(s*). In particular, if we fix s = s*, then for any y, f(y, s*) - g(s*) ≤ f(y*, s*) - g(s*). But since g(s*) is a constant when we fix s = s*, maximizing f(y, s*) over y is equivalent to maximizing [f(y, s*) - g(s*)] over y, because subtracting a constant doesn't change the location of the maximum. Therefore, the y that maximizes f(y, s*) should be the same y* that was part of the joint maximizer. Wait, is that right? Let me double-check. If we have f(y, s) - g(s) and we fix s = s*, then the problem becomes max_y [f(y, s*) - g(s*)]. Since g(s*) is fixed, the maximizer y must be the same as the one that maximizes f(y, s*). But in the joint optimization problem, when s is chosen as s*, the corresponding y that maximizes the expression is y*. Therefore, y* must be the maximizer of f(y, s*) over y. But hold on, is there a scenario where even though (y*, s*) is the joint maximizer, there could be another y that gives a higher f(y, s*) when s is fixed? Let's suppose, for contradiction, that there exists some y' ≠ y* such that f(y', s*) > f(y*, s*). Then, if we consider the joint problem, f(y', s*) - g(s*) would be greater than f(y*, s*) - g(s*), which contradicts the fact that (y*, s*) is the maximizer of the joint problem. Therefore, such a y' cannot exist, and thus y* must indeed be the maximizer of f(y, s*) over y. But the user mentioned that the maximizers are unique. That’s important. Suppose the joint maximizer is unique. Then, in the second problem, they also state that the maximizer is unique. So, uniqueness here is key. If there were multiple maximizers, maybe there could be different y's when you fix s at s*, but since they are unique, the reasoning above should hold. Let me think of an example. Let’s take simple functions where I can compute everything explicitly. Let 𝒴 and 𝒮 be real numbers. Suppose f(y, s) = - (y - s)^2, and g(s) = s^2. Then, the joint optimization problem is max_{y,s} [ - (y - s)^2 - s^2 ]. Let's compute this. First, expand the expression: - (y^2 - 2ys + s^2) - s^2 = -y^2 + 2ys - s^2 - s^2 = -y^2 + 2ys - 2s^2. To find the maximum, take partial derivatives with respect to y and s. ∂/∂y = -2y + 2s = 0 ⇒ y = s ∂/∂s = 2y - 4s = 0 From the first equation, y = s. Substitute into the second equation: 2s - 4s = -2s = 0 ⇒ s = 0. Therefore, y = 0. So, the unique maximizer is (y*, s*) = (0, 0). Now, let's fix s* = 0 and maximize f(y, 0) over y. f(y, 0) = - (y - 0)^2 = -y^2. The maximum is at y = 0, which is y*. So in this case, it works. Another example. Let f(y, s) = y*s and g(s) = s^2. But hold on, is there a scenario where even though (y*, s*) is the joint maximizer, there could be another y that gives a higher f(y, s*) when s is fixed? Then, if we consider the joint problem, f(y', s*) - g(s*) would be greater than f(y*, s*) - g(s*), which contradicts the fact that (y*, s*) is the maximizer of the joint problem. Therefore, such a y' cannot exist, and thus y* must indeed be the maximizer of f(y, s*) over y. But the user mentioned that the maximizers are unique. That’s important. Now, let's fix s* = 0 and maximize f(y, 0) over y. f(y, 0) = - (y - 0)^2 = -y^2. The maximum is at y = 0, which is y*. So in this case, it works. Another example. Let f(y, s) = y*s and g(s) = s^2. However, when we fix s*, we just maximize f(y, s*). But since (y*, s*) is the maximizer of f(y,s) - g(s), for that particular s*, the y that maximizes f(y,s*) must be y*, because if there was a better y for that s*, then the pair (y', s*) would give a higher value of f(y,s) - g(s), contradicting the optimality of (y*, s*). Therefore, the logic is that since (y*, s*) is the unique maximizer of the joint problem, then for s = s*, the optimal y is y*, otherwise, if there was a better y for s*, then (y', s*) would be a better pair, which can't happen. Therefore, yes, the maximizer of the second problem is y*. We need to determine if the maximizer of the problem \( \max_{y \in \mathcal{Y}} f(y, s^*) \) is equal to \( y^* \). 1. **Joint Optimization Problem**: The pair \( (y^*, s^*) \) is the unique maximizer of \( f(y, s) - g(s) \). This means for all other \( y \in \mathcal{Y} \) and \( s \in \mathcal{S} \), we have: \[ f(y, s) - g(s) \leq f(y^*, s^*) - g(s^*) \] 2. **Fixed \( s^* \) Optimization**: When fixing \( s = s^* \), we need to maximize \( f(y, s^*) \) over \( y \). Since \( g(s^*) \) is a constant, maximizing \( f(y, s^*) \) is equivalent to maximizing \( f(y, s^*) - g(s^*) \). 3. **Contradiction Argument**: Suppose there exists a \( y' \neq y^* \) such that \( f(y', s^*) > f(y^*, s^*) \). Then, the pair \( (y', s^*) \) would yield a higher value for the joint optimization problem: \[ f(y', s^*) - g(s^*) > f(y^*, s^*) - g(s^*) \] This contradicts the uniqueness of \( (y^*, s^*) \) as the maximizer of the joint problem. Therefore, no such \( y' \) can exist. 4. **Conclusion**: Since \( (y^*, s^*) \) is the unique maximizer of the joint problem, \( y^* \) must be the unique maximizer of \( f(y, s^*) \) when \( s \) is fixed at \( s^* \). Thus, the maximizer of the problem \( \max_{y \in \mathcal{Y}} f(y, s^*) \) is indeed \( y^* \). \[ \boxed{Yes} \]

Now consider the problem \( \max_{y \in \mathcal{Y}} f(y, s^*) \) with a unique maximizer. Is it true that the maximizer of this problem, \( \arg\max_{y \in \mathcal{Y}} f(y, s^*) \), is equal to $y^*$? Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

When we fix s* and then optimize over y, we are no longer considering the cost g(s), but in the original problem, s* was chosen to maximize f(y,s) - g(s). However, when we fix s*, we just maximize f(y, s*). But since (y*, s*) is the maximizer of f(y,s) - g(s), for that particular s*, the y that maximizes f(y,s*) must be y*, because if there was a better y for that s*, then the pair (y', s*) would give a higher value of f(y,s) - g(s), contradicting the optimality of (y*, s*). Therefore, the logic is that since (y*, s*) is the unique maximizer of the joint problem, then for s = s*, the optimal y is y*, otherwise, if there was a better y for s*, then (y', s*) would be a better pair, which can't happen. Therefore, yes, the maximizer of the second problem is y*. Therefore, the answer should be yes, it is true that the maximizer of the problem max_{y ∈ 𝒴} f(y, s*) is y*. **Final Answer** \boxed{Yes} </think> Given two unknowns \( y \in \mathcal{Y} \) and \( s \in \mathcal{S} \), we consider the joint optimization problem \( \max_{y \in \mathcal{Y}, s \in \mathcal{S}} f(y,s) - g(s) \) with unique maximizers \( (y^*, s^*) \). We need to determine if the maximizer of the problem \( \max_{y \in \mathcal{Y}} f(y, s^*) \) is equal to \( y^* \). 1. **Joint Optimization Problem**: The pair \( (y^*, s^*) \) is the unique maximizer of \( f(y, s) - g(s) \). This means for all other \( y \in \mathcal{Y} \) and \( s \in \mathcal{S} \), we have: \[ f(y, s) - g(s) \leq f(y^*, s^*) - g(s^*) \] 2. **Fixed \( s^* \) Optimization**: When fixing \( s = s^* \), we need to maximize \( f(y, s^*) \) over \( y \). Since \( g(s^*) \) is a constant, maximizing \( f(y, s^*) \) is equivalent to maximizing \( f(y, s^*) - g(s^*) \). 3. **Contradiction Argument**: Suppose there exists a \( y' \neq y^* \) such that \( f(y', s^*) > f(y^*, s^*) \). Then, the pair \( (y', s^*) \) would yield a higher value for the joint optimization problem: \[ f(y', s^*) - g(s^*) > f(y^*, s^*) - g(s^*) \] This contradicts the uniqueness of \( (y^*, s^*) \) as the maximizer of the joint problem. Therefore, no such \( y' \) can exist. 4. **Conclusion**: Since \( (y^*, s^*) \) is the unique maximizer of the joint problem, \( y^* \) must be the unique maximizer of \( f(y, s^*) \) when \( s \) is fixed at \( s^* \). Thus, the maximizer of the problem \( \max_{y \in \mathcal{Y}} f(y, s^*) \) is indeed \( y^* \). \[ \boxed{Yes} \]

Problem: Consider two unknowns $y \in \mathcal{Y}$ and $s \in \mathcal{S}$. Let $f$ be a function of $(y,s)$ and $g$ be a function of $s$. Suppose the maximizers of the problem \( \max_{y \in \mathcal{Y}, s \in \mathcal{S}} f(y,s) - g(s) \) are unique and denoted by $(y^*, s^*)$. Now consider the problem \( \max_{y \in \mathcal{Y}} f(y, s^*) \) with a unique maximizer. Is it true that the maximizer of this problem, \( \arg\max_{y \in \mathcal{Y}} f(y, s^*) \), is equal to $y^*$? Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: Yes \boxed{y^*}

Reference Answer: Alright, let's tackle this problem step by step. So, we have two unknowns, y and s, each in their own sets 𝒴 and 𝒮. The main goal is to maximize the function f(y, s) minus g(s). They've told us that the maximizers for this joint problem are unique and are denoted by (y*, s*). Then, we have another problem where we fix s at s* and just maximize f(y, s*) over y, and they want to know if the maximizer here is still y*. Hmm, okay. Let me think. So, the first thing that comes to mind is that when you maximize jointly over y and s, you're considering all possible combinations of y and s. So, (y*, s*) is the pair that gives the highest value of f(y,s) - g(s). Now, if we fix s at s* and then maximize over y, we're essentially looking for the best y given that s is already set to s*. But the question is whether this y will be the same y* as before. Intuitively, since s* was part of the optimal pair, maybe the y that was optimal when s was s* would still be optimal when we fix s at s*. But wait, is there any reason it might not be? Let me formalize this. Let's denote the joint optimization problem as: (y*, s*) = argmax_{y ∈ 𝒴, s ∈ 𝒮} [f(y, s) - g(s)] Then, the second problem is: y' = argmax_{y ∈ 𝒴} f(y, s*) We need to check if y' = y*. Since (y*, s*) is the unique maximizer of the joint problem, for all other y and s, f(y, s) - g(s) ≤ f(y*, s*) - g(s*). In particular, if we fix s = s*, then for any y, f(y, s*) - g(s*) ≤ f(y*, s*) - g(s*). But since g(s*) is a constant when we fix s = s*, maximizing f(y, s*) over y is equivalent to maximizing [f(y, s*) - g(s*)] over y, because subtracting a constant doesn't change the location of the maximum. Therefore, the y that maximizes f(y, s*) should be the same y* that was part of the joint maximizer. Wait, is that right? Let me double-check. If we have f(y, s) - g(s) and we fix s = s*, then the problem becomes max_y [f(y, s*) - g(s*)]. Since g(s*) is fixed, the maximizer y must be the same as the one that maximizes f(y, s*). But in the joint optimization problem, when s is chosen as s*, the corresponding y that maximizes the expression is y*. Therefore, y* must be the maximizer of f(y, s*) over y. But hold on, is there a scenario where even though (y*, s*) is the joint maximizer, there could be another y that gives a higher f(y, s*) when s is fixed? Let's suppose, for contradiction, that there exists some y' ≠ y* such that f(y', s*) > f(y*, s*). Then, if we consider the joint problem, f(y', s*) - g(s*) would be greater than f(y*, s*) - g(s*), which contradicts the fact that (y*, s*) is the maximizer of the joint problem. Therefore, such a y' cannot exist, and thus y* must indeed be the maximizer of f(y, s*) over y. But the user mentioned that the maximizers are unique. That’s important. Suppose the joint maximizer is unique. Then, in the second problem, they also state that the maximizer is unique. So, uniqueness here is key. If there were multiple maximizers, maybe there could be different y's when you fix s at s*, but since they are unique, the reasoning above should hold. Let me think of an example. Let’s take simple functions where I can compute everything explicitly. Let 𝒴 and 𝒮 be real numbers. Suppose f(y, s) = - (y - s)^2, and g(s) = s^2. Then, the joint optimization problem is max_{y,s} [ - (y - s)^2 - s^2 ]. Let's compute this. First, expand the expression: - (y^2 - 2ys + s^2) - s^2 = -y^2 + 2ys - s^2 - s^2 = -y^2 + 2ys - 2s^2. To find the maximum, take partial derivatives with respect to y and s. ∂/∂y = -2y + 2s = 0 ⇒ y = s ∂/∂s = 2y - 4s = 0 From the first equation, y = s. Substitute into the second equation: 2s - 4s = -2s = 0 ⇒ s = 0. Therefore, y = 0. So, the unique maximizer is (y*, s*) = (0, 0). Now, let's fix s* = 0 and maximize f(y, 0) over y. f(y, 0) = - (y - 0)^2 = -y^2. The maximum is at y = 0, which is y*. So in this case, it works. Another example. Let f(y, s) = y*s and g(s) = s^2. In particular, if we fix s = s*, then for any y, f(y, s*) - g(s*) ≤ f(y*, s*) - g(s*). But since g(s*) is a constant when we fix s = s*, maximizing f(y, s*) over y is equivalent to maximizing [f(y, s*) - g(s*)] over y, because subtracting a constant doesn't change the location of the maximum. Therefore, the y that maximizes f(y, s*) should be the same y* that was part of the joint maximizer. Wait, is that right? Let me double-check. We need to determine if the maximizer of the problem \( \max_{y \in \mathcal{Y}} f(y, s^*) \) is equal to \( y^* \). 1. **Joint Optimization Problem**: The pair \( (y^*, s^*) \) is the unique maximizer of \( f(y, s) - g(s) \). This means for all other \( y \in \mathcal{Y} \) and \( s \in \mathcal{S} \), we have: \[ f(y, s) - g(s) \leq f(y^*, s^*) - g(s^*) \] 2. **Fixed \( s^* \) Optimization**: When fixing \( s = s^* \), we need to maximize \( f(y, s^*) \) over \( y \). Since \( g(s^*) \) is a constant, maximizing \( f(y, s^*) \) is equivalent to maximizing \( f(y, s^*) - g(s^*) \). 3. **Contradiction Argument**: Suppose there exists a \( y' \neq y^* \) such that \( f(y', s^*) > f(y^*, s^*) \). Then, the pair \( (y', s^*) \) would yield a higher value for the joint optimization problem: \[ f(y', s^*) - g(s^*) > f(y^*, s^*) - g(s^*) \] This contradicts the uniqueness of \( (y^*, s^*) \) as the maximizer of the joint problem. Therefore, no such \( y' \) can exist. 4. **Conclusion**: Since \( (y^*, s^*) \) is the unique maximizer of the joint problem, \( y^* \) must be the unique maximizer of \( f(y, s^*) \) when \( s \) is fixed at \( s^* \). Thus, the maximizer of the problem \( \max_{y \in \mathcal{Y}} f(y, s^*) \) is indeed \( y^* \). \[ \boxed{Yes} \]

Then, fix s* =1 and maximize f(y, 1) = y - 0 = y. So maximizer is y=1, which is y*. But what if the functions are more intertwined? Suppose f(y, s) = -(y - s)^2 and g(s) = -(s - a)^2 for some a. Wait, then the joint maximization would be max_{y,s} [ -(y - s)^2 + (s - a)^2 ]. Let’s compute this. Expanding: -y² + 2ys - s² + s² - 2as + a² = -y² + 2ys - 2as + a². Take partial derivatives: ∂/∂y = -2y + 2s = 0 ⇒ y = s ∂/∂s = 2y - 2a = 0 ⇒ y = a Thus, y = s = a. So (y*, s*) = (a, a). Then, if we fix s* = a and maximize f(y, a) = -(y - a)^2, which is maximized at y = a, which is y*. So again, it works. Wait, maybe the answer is yes? But the question seems to suggest there might be a trick. Let me think again. Suppose that in the joint optimization problem, the optimal s* is chosen considering the cost g(s). When we fix s* and then optimize over y, we are no longer considering the cost g(s), but in the original problem, s* was chosen to maximize f(y,s) - g(s). However, when we fix s*, we just maximize f(y, s*). But since (y*, s*) is the maximizer of f(y,s) - g(s), for that particular s*, the y that maximizes f(y,s*) must be y*, because if there was a better y for that s*, then the pair (y', s*) would give a higher value of f(y,s) - g(s), contradicting the optimality of (y*, s*). Therefore, the logic is that since (y*, s*) is the unique maximizer of the joint problem, then for s = s*, the optimal y is y*, otherwise, if there was a better y for s*, then (y', s*) would be a better pair, which can't happen. Therefore, yes, the maximizer of the second problem is y*. Therefore, the answer should be yes, it is true that the maximizer of the problem max_{y ∈ 𝒴} f(y, s*) is y*. **Final Answer** \boxed{Yes} </think> Given two unknowns \( y \in \mathcal{Y} \) and \( s \in \mathcal{S} \), we consider the joint optimization problem \( \max_{y \in \mathcal{Y}, s \in \mathcal{S}} f(y,s) - g(s) \) with unique maximizers \( (y^*, s^*) \). We need to determine if the maximizer of the problem \( \max_{y \in \mathcal{Y}} f(y, s^*) \) is equal to \( y^* \). 1. **Joint Optimization Problem**: The pair \( (y^*, s^*) \) is the unique maximizer of \( f(y, s) - g(s) \). This means for all other \( y \in \mathcal{Y} \) and \( s \in \mathcal{S} \), we have: \[ f(y, s) - g(s) \leq f(y^*, s^*) - g(s^*) \] 2. **Fixed \( s^* \) Optimization**: When fixing \( s = s^* \), we need to maximize \( f(y, s^*) \) over \( y \). Since \( g(s^*) \) is a constant, maximizing \( f(y, s^*) \) is equivalent to maximizing \( f(y, s^*) - g(s^*) \). 3. **Contradiction Argument**: Suppose there exists a \( y' \neq y^* \) such that \( f(y', s^*) > f(y^*, s^*) \). Then, the pair \( (y', s^*) \) would yield a higher value for the joint optimization problem: \[ f(y', s^*) - g(s^*) > f(y^*, s^*) - g(s^*) \] This contradicts the uniqueness of \( (y^*, s^*) \) as the maximizer of the joint problem. Therefore, no such \( y' \) can exist. 4. **Conclusion**: Since \( (y^*, s^*) \) is the unique maximizer of the joint problem, \( y^* \) must be the unique maximizer of \( f(y, s^*) \) when \( s \) is fixed at \( s^* \). Thus, the maximizer of the problem \( \max_{y \in \mathcal{Y}} f(y, s^*) \) is indeed \( y^* \). \[ \boxed{Yes} \]

Problem: Consider two unknowns $y \in \mathcal{Y}$ and $s \in \mathcal{S}$. Let $f$ be a function of $(y,s)$ and $g$ be a function of $s$. Suppose the maximizers of the problem \( \max_{y \in \mathcal{Y}, s \in \mathcal{S}} f(y,s) - g(s) \) are unique and denoted by $(y^*, s^*)$. Now consider the problem \( \max_{y \in \mathcal{Y}} f(y, s^*) \) with a unique maximizer. Is it true that the maximizer of this problem, \( \arg\max_{y \in \mathcal{Y}} f(y, s^*) \), is equal to $y^*$? Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: Yes \boxed{y^*}

Reference Answer: Alright, let's tackle this problem step by step. So, we have two unknowns, y and s, each in their own sets 𝒴 and 𝒮. The main goal is to maximize the function f(y, s) minus g(s). They've told us that the maximizers for this joint problem are unique and are denoted by (y*, s*). Then, we have another problem where we fix s at s* and just maximize f(y, s*) over y, and they want to know if the maximizer here is still y*. Hmm, okay. Let me think. So, the first thing that comes to mind is that when you maximize jointly over y and s, you're considering all possible combinations of y and s. So, (y*, s*) is the pair that gives the highest value of f(y,s) - g(s). Now, if we fix s at s* and then maximize over y, we're essentially looking for the best y given that s is already set to s*. But the question is whether this y will be the same y* as before. Intuitively, since s* was part of the optimal pair, maybe the y that was optimal when s was s* would still be optimal when we fix s at s*. But wait, is there any reason it might not be? Let me formalize this. Let's denote the joint optimization problem as: (y*, s*) = argmax_{y ∈ 𝒴, s ∈ 𝒮} [f(y, s) - g(s)] Then, the second problem is: y' = argmax_{y ∈ 𝒴} f(y, s*) We need to check if y' = y*. Since (y*, s*) is the unique maximizer of the joint problem, for all other y and s, f(y, s) - g(s) ≤ f(y*, s*) - g(s*). In particular, if we fix s = s*, then for any y, f(y, s*) - g(s*) ≤ f(y*, s*) - g(s*). But since g(s*) is a constant when we fix s = s*, maximizing f(y, s*) over y is equivalent to maximizing [f(y, s*) - g(s*)] over y, because subtracting a constant doesn't change the location of the maximum. Therefore, the y that maximizes f(y, s*) should be the same y* that was part of the joint maximizer. Wait, is that right? Let me double-check. If we have f(y, s) - g(s) and we fix s = s*, then the problem becomes max_y [f(y, s*) - g(s*)]. Since g(s*) is fixed, the maximizer y must be the same as the one that maximizes f(y, s*). But in the joint optimization problem, when s is chosen as s*, the corresponding y that maximizes the expression is y*. Therefore, y* must be the maximizer of f(y, s*) over y. But hold on, is there a scenario where even though (y*, s*) is the joint maximizer, there could be another y that gives a higher f(y, s*) when s is fixed? Let's suppose, for contradiction, that there exists some y' ≠ y* such that f(y', s*) > f(y*, s*). Then, if we consider the joint problem, f(y', s*) - g(s*) would be greater than f(y*, s*) - g(s*), which contradicts the fact that (y*, s*) is the maximizer of the joint problem. Therefore, such a y' cannot exist, and thus y* must indeed be the maximizer of f(y, s*) over y. But the user mentioned that the maximizers are unique. That’s important. Suppose the joint maximizer is unique. Then, in the second problem, they also state that the maximizer is unique. So, uniqueness here is key. If there were multiple maximizers, maybe there could be different y's when you fix s at s*, but since they are unique, the reasoning above should hold. Let me think of an example. Let’s take simple functions where I can compute everything explicitly. Let 𝒴 and 𝒮 be real numbers. Suppose f(y, s) = - (y - s)^2, and g(s) = s^2. Then, the joint optimization problem is max_{y,s} [ - (y - s)^2 - s^2 ]. Let's compute this. First, expand the expression: - (y^2 - 2ys + s^2) - s^2 = -y^2 + 2ys - s^2 - s^2 = -y^2 + 2ys - 2s^2. To find the maximum, take partial derivatives with respect to y and s. ∂/∂y = -2y + 2s = 0 ⇒ y = s ∂/∂s = 2y - 4s = 0 From the first equation, y = s. Substitute into the second equation: 2s - 4s = -2s = 0 ⇒ s = 0. Therefore, y = 0. So, the unique maximizer is (y*, s*) = (0, 0). Now, let's fix s* = 0 and maximize f(y, 0) over y. f(y, 0) = - (y - 0)^2 = -y^2. The maximum is at y = 0, which is y*. So in this case, it works. Another example. Let f(y, s) = y*s and g(s) = s^2. Suppose 𝒴 and 𝒮 are real numbers. Then the joint optimization is max_{y,s} [ y*s - s^2 ]. Let's compute this. Problem: Find a polynomial $f(x)$ such that the ring $\mathbb{Q}[x]/(f)$ is isomorphic to the set $R = \{ a + b\sqrt{2} : a,b \in \mathbb{Q} \}$. What is the polynomial $f(x)$? Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: x^2 - 2 \boxed{0}

Reference Answer: Alright, so I need to find a polynomial f(x) such that the ring Q[x]/(f) is isomorphic to the set R, which consists of numbers of the form a + b√2 where a and b are rational numbers. Hmm, okay. Let me start by recalling some concepts from ring theory and field extensions. First, I know that Q[x]/(f(x)) is a quotient ring, where we take the ring of polynomials with rational coefficients and mod out by the ideal generated by the polynomial f(x). This quotient ring is a field if and only if f(x) is irreducible over Q. Since R is a field (because we can invert any non-zero element of the form a + b√2), I need f(x) to be irreducible over Q. Now, R is essentially the field extension Q(√2). So, the problem reduces to finding the minimal polynomial of √2 over Q, because the quotient ring Q[x]/(f(x)) will be isomorphic to Q(√2) if f(x) is the minimal polynomial of √2. What's the minimal polynomial of √2 over Q? Well, √2 is a root of the polynomial x² - 2. Let me check if this polynomial is irreducible over Q. By Eisenstein's criterion with prime p = 2, the polynomial x² - 2 is irreducible because 2 divides the constant term -2, 2² = 4 does not divide -2, and 2 doesn't divide the leading coefficient 1. So yes, x² - 2 is irreducible over Q. Therefore, Q(√2) is isomorphic to Q[x]/(x² - 2). Wait, but let me make sure. If I take Q[x]/(x² - 2), then every element in this quotient ring can be written as a linear combination of 1 and x, right? Because when we mod out by x² - 2, any higher degree terms can be reduced using x² = 2. So, elements are of the form a + bx + (x² - 2), which simplifies to a + bx. Then, mapping x to √2 gives an isomorphism to R, which is Q(√2). So, yes, that seems right. But let me double-check. Suppose I have a ring homomorphism φ: Q[x] → R defined by φ(p(x)) = p(√2). Then, the kernel of φ is the set of all polynomials p(x) such that p(√2) = 0. The kernel is precisely the ideal generated by the minimal polynomial of √2, which is x² - 2. Therefore, by the first isomorphism theorem, Q[x]/(x² - 2) is isomorphic to R. That makes sense. So, f(x) should be x² - 2. Let me see if there could be another polynomial. Suppose I choose a different polynomial, say, of higher degree. For example, suppose I take (x² - 2)³. Then Q[x]/( (x² - 2)³ ) would not be a field because the ideal generated by a reducible polynomial is not maximal. But R is a field, so f(x) must be irreducible, hence minimal polynomial. So x² - 2 is indeed the minimal polynomial, so that's the one. Alternatively, could there be another irreducible polynomial of degree 2 that √2 satisfies? Let's think. Suppose there's another quadratic polynomial with rational coefficients that has √2 as a root. Then, since x² - 2 is irreducible, any other such polynomial would have to be a scalar multiple of x² - 2. Because in Q[x], the minimal polynomial is unique up to multiplication by a non-zero constant. So, if we want the monic polynomial, which is x² - 2, that's the generator of the ideal. Therefore, f(x) = x² - 2 is the polynomial we're looking for. Let me check if I made any mistakes here. Suppose someone suggests f(x) = x - √2. But that's not a polynomial with rational coefficients. The polynomial must be in Q[x], so coefficients must be rational. So, x - √2 is not in Q[x], so that's invalid. Alternatively, maybe a linear polynomial? But √2 is not rational, so there's no linear polynomial in Q[x] with √2 as a root. Therefore, the minimal degree is 2, so f(x) must be quadratic. And the only monic irreducible quadratic polynomial with root √2 is x² - 2. Therefore, the answer is x² - 2. **Final Answer** The polynomial \( f(x) \) is \boxed{x^2 - 2}. </think> To find a polynomial \( f(x) \) such that the ring \( \mathbb{Q}[x]/(f) \) is isomorphic to the set \( R = \{ a + b\sqrt{2} : a, b \in \mathbb{Q} \} \), we start by recognizing that \( R \) is the field \( \mathbb{Q}(\sqrt{2}) \). The key step is to determine the minimal polynomial of \( \sqrt{2} \) over \( \mathbb{Q} \). The polynomial \( x^2 - 2 \) has \( \sqrt{2} \) as a root and is irreducible over \( \mathbb{Q} \) by Eisenstein's criterion with the prime \( p = 2 \). This polynomial is the minimal polynomial because it is irreducible and of the lowest degree with \( \sqrt{2} \) as a root. The quotient ring \( \mathbb{Q}[x]/(x^2 - 2) \) consists of equivalence classes of polynomials modulo \( x^2 - 2 \). Each equivalence class can be represented by a linear polynomial \( a + bx \), which corresponds directly to an element \( a + b\sqrt{2} \) in \( R \). The isomorphism is confirmed by mapping \( x \) to \( \sqrt{2} \), showing that \( \mathbb{Q}[x]/(x^2 - 2) \) is indeed isomorphic to \( \mathbb{Q}(\sqrt{2}) \). Thus, the polynomial \( f(x) \) is \(\boxed{x^2 - 2}\).

Reference Answer: Alright, so I need to find a polynomial f(x) such that the ring Q[x]/(f) is isomorphic to the set R, which consists of numbers of the form a + b√2 where a and b are rational numbers. Hmm, okay. Let me start by recalling some concepts from ring theory and field extensions. First, I know that Q[x]/(f(x)) is a quotient ring, where we take the ring of polynomials with rational coefficients and mod out by the ideal generated by the polynomial f(x). This quotient ring is a field if and only if f(x) is irreducible over Q. Since R is a field (because we can invert any non-zero element of the form a + b√2), I need f(x) to be irreducible over Q. Now, R is essentially the field extension Q(√2). So, the problem reduces to finding the minimal polynomial of √2 over Q, because the quotient ring Q[x]/(f(x)) will be isomorphic to Q(√2) if f(x) is the minimal polynomial of √2. What's the minimal polynomial of √2 over Q? Well, √2 is a root of the polynomial x² - 2. Let me check if this polynomial is irreducible over Q. By Eisenstein's criterion with prime p = 2, the polynomial x² - 2 is irreducible because 2 divides the constant term -2, 2² = 4 does not divide -2, and 2 doesn't divide the leading coefficient 1. So yes, x² - 2 is irreducible over Q. Therefore, Q(√2) is isomorphic to Q[x]/(x² - 2). Wait, but let me make sure. If I take Q[x]/(x² - 2), then every element in this quotient ring can be written as a linear combination of 1 and x, right? Because when we mod out by x² - 2, any higher degree terms can be reduced using x² = 2. So, elements are of the form a + bx + (x² - 2), which simplifies to a + bx. Then, mapping x to √2 gives an isomorphism to R, which is Q(√2). So, yes, that seems right. But let me double-check. Suppose I have a ring homomorphism φ: Q[x] → R defined by φ(p(x)) = p(√2). Then, the kernel of φ is the set of all polynomials p(x) such that p(√2) = 0. The kernel is precisely the ideal generated by the minimal polynomial of √2, which is x² - 2. Therefore, by the first isomorphism theorem, Q[x]/(x² - 2) is isomorphic to R. That makes sense. So, f(x) should be x² - 2. Let me see if there could be another polynomial. Suppose I choose a different polynomial, say, of higher degree. For example, suppose I take (x² - 2)³. Then Q[x]/( (x² - 2)³ ) would not be a field because the ideal generated by a reducible polynomial is not maximal. But R is a field, so f(x) must be irreducible, hence minimal polynomial. So x² - 2 is indeed the minimal polynomial, so that's the one. Alternatively, could there be another irreducible polynomial of degree 2 that √2 satisfies? Let's think. Suppose there's another quadratic polynomial with rational coefficients that has √2 as a root. Then, since x² - 2 is irreducible, any other such polynomial would have to be a scalar multiple of x² - 2. Because in Q[x], the minimal polynomial is unique up to multiplication by a non-zero constant. So, if we want the monic polynomial, which is x² - 2, that's the generator of the ideal. Therefore, f(x) = x² - 2 is the polynomial we're looking for. Let me check if I made any mistakes here. Suppose someone suggests f(x) = x - √2. But that's not a polynomial with rational coefficients. The polynomial must be in Q[x], so coefficients must be rational. So, x - √2 is not in Q[x], so that's invalid. Alternatively, maybe a linear polynomial? But √2 is not rational, so there's no linear polynomial in Q[x] with √2 as a root. Therefore, the minimal degree is 2, so f(x) must be quadratic. And the only monic irreducible quadratic polynomial with root √2 is x² - 2. Therefore, the answer is x² - 2. **Final Answer** The polynomial \( f(x) \) is \boxed{x^2 - 2}. </think> To find a polynomial \( f(x) \) such that the ring \( \mathbb{Q}[x]/(f) \) is isomorphic to the set \( R = \{ a + b\sqrt{2} : a, b \in \mathbb{Q} \} \), we start by recognizing that \( R \) is the field \( \mathbb{Q}(\sqrt{2}) \). The key step is to determine the minimal polynomial of \( \sqrt{2} \) over \( \mathbb{Q} \). The polynomial \( x^2 - 2 \) has \( \sqrt{2} \) as a root and is irreducible over \( \mathbb{Q} \) by Eisenstein's criterion with the prime \( p = 2 \). This polynomial is the minimal polynomial because it is irreducible and of the lowest degree with \( \sqrt{2} \) as a root. The quotient ring \( \mathbb{Q}[x]/(x^2 - 2) \) consists of equivalence classes of polynomials modulo \( x^2 - 2 \). Each equivalence class can be represented by a linear polynomial \( a + bx \), which corresponds directly to an element \( a + b\sqrt{2} \) in \( R \). The isomorphism is confirmed by mapping \( x \) to \( \sqrt{2} \), showing that \( \mathbb{Q}[x]/(x^2 - 2) \) is indeed isomorphic to \( \mathbb{Q}(\sqrt{2}) \). Thus, the polynomial \( f(x) \) is \(\boxed{x^2 - 2}\).

If I take Q[x]/(x² - 2), then every element in this quotient ring can be written as a linear combination of 1 and x, right? Because when we mod out by x² - 2, any higher degree terms can be reduced using x² = 2. So, elements are of the form a + bx + (x² - 2), which simplifies to a + bx. Then, mapping x to √2 gives an isomorphism to R, which is Q(√2). So, yes, that seems right. But let me double-check. Suppose I have a ring homomorphism φ: Q[x] → R defined by φ(p(x)) = p(√2). Then, the kernel of φ is the set of all polynomials p(x) such that p(√2) = 0. The kernel is precisely the ideal generated by the minimal polynomial of √2, which is x² - 2. Therefore, by the first isomorphism theorem, Q[x]/(x² - 2) is isomorphic to R. That makes sense. So, f(x) should be x² - 2. Let me see if there could be another polynomial. Suppose I choose a different polynomial, say, of higher degree. For example, suppose I take (x² - 2)³. Then Q[x]/( (x² - 2)³ ) would not be a field because the ideal generated by a reducible polynomial is not maximal. But R is a field, so f(x) must be irreducible, hence minimal polynomial. So x² - 2 is indeed the minimal polynomial, so that's the one. Alternatively, could there be another irreducible polynomial of degree 2 that √2 satisfies? Let's think. Suppose there's another quadratic polynomial with rational coefficients that has √2 as a root. Then, since x² - 2 is irreducible, any other such polynomial would have to be a scalar multiple of x² - 2. Suppose there's another quadratic polynomial with rational coefficients that has √2 as a root. Then, since x² - 2 is irreducible, any other such polynomial would have to be a scalar multiple of x² - 2. Because in Q[x], the minimal polynomial is unique up to multiplication by a non-zero constant. So, if we want the monic polynomial, which is x² - 2, that's the generator of the ideal. Therefore, f(x) = x² - 2 is the polynomial we're looking for. Let me check if I made any mistakes here. Suppose someone suggests f(x) = x - √2. But that's not a polynomial with rational coefficients. The polynomial must be in Q[x], so coefficients must be rational. So, x - √2 is not in Q[x], so that's invalid. Alternatively, maybe a linear polynomial? But √2 is not rational, so there's no linear polynomial in Q[x] with √2 as a root. Therefore, the minimal degree is 2, so f(x) must be quadratic. And the only monic irreducible quadratic polynomial with root √2 is x² - 2. Therefore, the answer is x² - 2. **Final Answer** The polynomial \( f(x) \) is \boxed{x^2 - 2}. </think> To find a polynomial \( f(x) \) such that the ring \( \mathbb{Q}[x]/(f) \) is isomorphic to the set \( R = \{ a + b\sqrt{2} : a, b \in \mathbb{Q} \} \), we start by recognizing that \( R \) is the field \( \mathbb{Q}(\sqrt{2}) \). The key step is to determine the minimal polynomial of \( \sqrt{2} \) over \( \mathbb{Q} \). The polynomial \( x^2 - 2 \) has \( \sqrt{2} \) as a root and is irreducible over \( \mathbb{Q} \) by Eisenstein's criterion with the prime \( p = 2 \). This polynomial is the minimal polynomial because it is irreducible and of the lowest degree with \( \sqrt{2} \) as a root. The quotient ring \( \mathbb{Q}[x]/(x^2 - 2) \) consists of equivalence classes of polynomials modulo \( x^2 - 2 \). Each equivalence class can be represented by a linear polynomial \( a + bx \), which corresponds directly to an element \( a + b\sqrt{2} \) in \( R \). The isomorphism is confirmed by mapping \( x \) to \( \sqrt{2} \), showing that \( \mathbb{Q}[x]/(x^2 - 2) \) is indeed isomorphic to \( \mathbb{Q}(\sqrt{2}) \). Thus, the polynomial \( f(x) \) is \(\boxed{x^2 - 2}\).

do_single_rollout

Problem: Let $x,$ $y,$ and $z$ be angles such that \begin{align*} \cos x &= \tan y, \\ \cos y &= \tan z, \\ \cos z &= \tan x. \end{align*}Find the largest possible value of $\sin x.$ Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: \boxed{1}

Reference Answer: From $\cos x = \tan y,$ \[\cos^2 x = \tan^2 y = \frac{\sin^2 y}{\cos^2 y} = \frac{1 - \cos^2 y}{\cos^2 y} = \frac{1}{\cos^2 y} - 1.\]Since $\cos y = \tan z,$ $\cos^2 x = \cot^2 y - 1.$ Then \[1 + \cos^2 x = \cot^2 z = \frac{\cos^2 z}{\sin^2 z} = \frac{\cos^2 z}{1 - \cos^2 z}.\]Since $\cos z = \tan x,$ \[1 + \cos^2 x = \frac{\tan^2 x}{1 - \tan^2 x} = \frac{\sin^2 x}{\cos^2 x - \sin^2 x}.\]We can write this as \[1 + (1 - \sin^2 x) = \frac{\sin^2 x}{(1 - \sin^2 x) - \sin^2 x},\]so $(2 - \sin^2 x)(1 - 2 \sin^2 x) = \sin^2 x.$ This simplifies to \[\sin^4 x - 3 \sin^2 x + 1 = 0.\]We recognize this as a quadratic in $\sin^2 x$: $(\sin^2 x)^2 - 3 \sin^2 x + 1 = 0.$ Then by the quadratic formula, \[\sin^2 x = \frac{3 \pm \sqrt{5}}{2}.\]Since $\frac{3 + \sqrt{5}}{2} > 1,$ we must have \[\sin^2 x = \frac{3 - \sqrt{5}}{2}.\]We guess that $\sin x$ is of the form $a + b \sqrt{5},$ for some numbers $a$ and $b.$ Thus, \[(a + b \sqrt{5})^2 = \frac{3 - \sqrt{5}}{2} = \frac{3}{2} - \frac{1}{2} \sqrt{5}.\]Expanding, we get \[a^2 + 5b^2 + 2ab \sqrt{5} = \frac{3}{2} - \frac{1}{2} \sqrt{5}.\]We set $a^2 + 5b^2 = \frac{3}{2}$ and $2ab = -\frac{1}{2}.$ Then $ab = -\frac{1}{4},$ so $b = -\frac{1}{4a}.$ Substituting into $a^2 + 5b^2 = \frac{3}{2},$ we get \[a^2 + \frac{5}{16a^2} = \frac{3}{2}.\]Then $16a^4 + 5 = 24a^2,$ so $16a^4 - 24a^2 + 5 = 0.$ This factors as $(4a^2 - 1)(4a^2 - 5) = 0.$ Thus, possible values of $a$ are $\pm \frac{1}{2}.$ Then $b = \mp \frac{1}{2},$ so \[\sin x = \pm \frac{1 - \sqrt{5}}{2}.\]Let \[\theta = \arcsin a,\]where $a = \frac{\sqrt{5} - 1}{2}.$ Note that $a$ satisfies $a^2 + a - 1 = 0.$ Then \begin{align*} \cos \theta - \tan \theta &= \cos \theta - \frac{\sin \theta}{\cos \theta} \\ &= \frac{\cos^2 \theta - \sin \theta}{\cos \theta} \\ &= \frac{1 - \sin^2 \theta - \sin \theta}{\cos \theta} \\ &= \frac{1 - a^2 - a}{\cos \theta} = 0. \end{align*}Thus, $(x,y,z) = (\theta, \theta, \theta)$ is a solution to the given system, which means the largest possible value of $\sin x$ is $\boxed{\frac{\sqrt{5} - 1}{2}}.$

Format Valid: ✓, Correct: ✗, Reward: 0.00

\sqrt{5}}{2}.\]We guess that $\sin x$ is of the form $a + b \sqrt{5},$ for some numbers $a$ and $b.$ Thus, \[(a + b \sqrt{5})^2 = \frac{3 - \sqrt{5}}{2} = \frac{3}{2} - \frac{1}{2} \sqrt{5}.\]Expanding, we get \[a^2 + 5b^2 + 2ab \sqrt{5} = \frac{3}{2} - \frac{1}{2} \sqrt{5}.\]We set $a^2 + 5b^2 = \frac{3}{2}$ and $2ab = -\frac{1}{2}.$ Then $ab = -\frac{1}{4},$ so $b = -\frac{1}{4a}.$ Substituting into $a^2 + 5b^2 = \frac{3}{2},$ we get \[a^2 + \frac{5}{16a^2} = \frac{3}{2}.\]Then $16a^4 + 5 = 24a^2,$ so $16a^4 - 24a^2 + 5 = 0.$ This factors as $(4a^2 - 1)(4a^2 - 5) = 0.$ Thus, possible values of $a$ are $\pm \frac{1}{2}.$ Then $b = \mp \frac{1}{2},$ so \[\sin x = \pm \frac{1 - \sqrt{5}}{2}.\]Let \[\theta = \arcsin a,\]where $a = \frac{\sqrt{5} - 1}{2}.$ Note that $a$ satisfies $a^2 + a - 1 = 0.$ Then \begin{align*} \cos \theta - \tan \theta &= \cos \theta - \frac{\sin \theta}{\cos \theta} \\ &= \frac{\cos^2 \theta - \sin \theta}{\cos \theta} \\ &= \frac{1 - \sin^2 \theta - \sin \theta}{\cos \theta} \\ &= \frac{1 - a^2 - a}{\cos \theta} = 0. \end{align*}Thus, $(x,y,z) = (\theta, \theta, \theta)$ is a solution to the given system, which means the largest possible value of $\sin x$ is $\boxed{\frac{\sqrt{5} - 1}{2}}.$

Format Valid: ✓, Correct: ✗, Reward: 0.00

Problem: Let $x,$ $y,$ and $z$ be angles such that \begin{align*} \cos x &= \tan y, \\ \cos y &= \tan z, \\ \cos z &= \tan x. \end{align*}Find the largest possible value of $\sin x.$ Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: \boxed{1}

Reference Answer: From $\cos x = \tan y,$ \[\cos^2 x = \tan^2 y = \frac{\sin^2 y}{\cos^2 y} = \frac{1 - \cos^2 y}{\cos^2 y} = \frac{1}{\cos^2 y} - 1.\]Since $\cos y = \tan z,$ $\cos^2 x = \cot^2 y - 1.$ Then \[1 + \cos^2 x = \cot^2 z = \frac{\cos^2 z}{\sin^2 z} = \frac{\cos^2 z}{1 - \cos^2 z}.\]Since $\cos z = \tan x,$ \[1 + \cos^2 x = \frac{\tan^2 x}{1 - \tan^2 x} = \frac{\sin^2 x}{\cos^2 x - \sin^2 x}.\]We can write this as \[1 + (1 - \sin^2 x) = \frac{\sin^2 x}{(1 - \sin^2 x) - \sin^2 x},\]so $(2 - \sin^2 x)(1 - 2 \sin^2 x) = \sin^2 x.$ This simplifies to \[\sin^4 x - 3 \sin^2 x + 1 = 0.\]We recognize this as a quadratic in $\sin^2 x$: $(\sin^2 x)^2 - 3 \sin^2 x + 1 = 0.$ Then by the quadratic formula, \[\sin^2 x = \frac{3 \pm \sqrt{5}}{2}.\]Since $\frac{3 + \sqrt{5}}{2} > 1,$ we must have \[\sin^2 x = \frac{3 - \sqrt{5}}{2}.\]We guess that $\sin x$ is of the form $a + b \sqrt{5},$ for some numbers $a$ and $b.$ Thus, \[(a + b \sqrt{5})^2 = \frac{3 - \sqrt{5}}{2} = \frac{3}{2} - \frac{1}{2} \sqrt{5}.\]Expanding, we get \[a^2 + 5b^2 + 2ab \sqrt{5} = \frac{3}{2} - \frac{1}{2} \sqrt{5}.\]We set $a^2 + 5b^2 = \frac{3}{2}$ and $2ab = -\frac{1}{2}.$ Then $ab = -\frac{1}{4},$ so $b = -\frac{1}{4a}.$ Substituting into $a^2 + 5b^2 = \frac{3}{2},$ we get \[a^2 + \frac{5}{16a^2} = \frac{3}{2}.\]Then $16a^4 + 5 = 24a^2,$ so $16a^4 - 24a^2 + 5 = 0.$ This factors as $(4a^2 - 1)(4a^2 - 5) = 0.$ Thus, possible values of $a$ are $\pm \frac{1}{2}.$ Then $b = \mp \frac{1}{2},$ so \[\sin x = \pm \frac{1 - \sqrt{5}}{2}.\]Let \[\theta = \arcsin a,\]where $a = \frac{\sqrt{5} - 1}{2}.$ Note that $a$ satisfies $a^2 + a - 1 = 0.$ Then \begin{align*} \cos \theta - \tan \theta &= \cos \theta - \frac{\sin \theta}{\cos \theta} \\ &= \frac{\cos^2 \theta - \sin \theta}{\cos \theta} \\ &= \frac{1 - \sin^2 \theta - \sin \theta}{\cos \theta} \\ &= \frac{1 - a^2 - a}{\cos \theta} = 0. \end{align*}Thus, $(x,y,z) = (\theta, \theta, \theta)$ is a solution to the given system, which means the largest possible value of $\sin x$ is $\boxed{\frac{\sqrt{5} - 1}{2}}.$

Format Valid: ✓, Correct: ✗, Reward: 0.00
